Refutation of the Dualist Claim of Two Eternal Principles
Maturidi refutes the Manichaean doctrine of light and darkness as co-eternal principles leading to the world via mixture.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's critique of the Manichaean belief that light and darkness are co-eternal principles whose mixture produced the world.

Core Idea
Mixture is temporal, so a world from mixture cannot be eternal.

Having established that temporal events cannot be eternal, Maturidi now applies this principle to the dualist claim that the world originates from the mixture of two eternal principles: light and darkness. He argues that since the mixture itself is a temporal event, the resulting world cannot be eternal. He then exposes further absurdities in the specific version of Mani.

Guided Path
1
Read the dualist claim
Identify the dualist statement: that light and darkness were separate, then mixed, and the world came from that mixture.
Note the terms 'الظلة' (likely 'الظلمة' darkness) and 'النور' light.
2
Understand Maturidi's key objection
The mixture is temporal because it follows a state of separation. Therefore, the world that arises from the mixture cannot be eternal, contradicting the claim of an eternal world.
3
Analyze the reductio ad absurdum
Maturidi shows that Mani's version leads to absurdities: light was powerless to prevent the attack, ignorant of the timing, and needed to create a world to free itself—contradicting its divinity.
This is a rhetorical refutation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وما ذكر من التوهم فإنه قد يتوهم في كل شيخ - في أول ما شاخ بقدميه، ولم يجب به الوصف في الأزل به الوصف في الأزل، وكذا في كل حركة وسكون، وتفرق واجتماع. فإن قلت: ذا محال، فمثله كون الحدث في الأزل محال، والله الموفق: ازعم من يقول بالإثنين - الظلة والنور - بقدم العالم، وأحق من يأبى ذلك من بقول بهذا؛ إذ من قولهم إنهما كانا متباينين فامتزجا، فكان العالم من امتزاجها. ومعلوم أن الإمتزاج كان حادثاً، إذ التباين كان هو المتقدم ولم يكونا يلقيان بالعالم. إلا أن يقولوا: النور والظلمة جوهران اختلفا، في الأصل بمكانهما، فكان مكان النوير نور كله وخير، ومكان الظلمة ظلمة كلها وشر، فيبطل القول بقدم العالم الممتزج، وبخاصة قول الماني حيث زعم أن النور لما رأى الظلمة قدحت فيه ومازجت به أحدث هذا العالم؛ ليتخلص بذلك أجزاء النور من أجزاء الظلمه، فصار العالم على هذا القول بعد الامتزاج المحدث مكون بعد المحدث قديما. وذلك [هو] التجاهل. فأوجبوا عجز النور وقت كونه في سلطانه بجميع أعوانه من الخيرات وأنصاره من الحسنات حيث لم يقدر على الإمتناع من قدح الظلمة وأحد احزابه عنه، وجهلوه بوقت القدح فيه ليتخلص منه، ثم وزعموا أنه أحدث هذا العالم ليخلص أجزاءه منه بعد أن صبار في وثاقها - هيهات ما أبعدهم عن ذلك، وما أجهل من يقدمونه ويجعلون له كل وأول كل خير وعلم، وقد جهل ما ذكر، وعظم كل خير بقوة، وقد عجز.
And what was mentioned of misconception—for one might be deluded regarding every old man: at the beginning of his aging with his feet, and the description in eternity is not necessitated by it, similarly in every movement and rest, separation and combination. For if you say: this is impossible, then likewise the occurrence of a temporal event in eternity is impossible. And God is the granter of success: I think that he who says there are two [principles]—darkness and light—[affirms] the pre-eternity of the world, and he who rejects that is more worthy by saying this; for from their sayings is that they were separate, then mixed, so the world came from their mixture. And it is known that the mixture was temporal, since separation was prior, and they were not producing the world. Unless they say: light and darkness are two substances that differed originally in their places, so the place of light was all light and good, and the place of darkness all darkness and evil; then the saying of the pre-eternity of the mixed world is invalidated, especially the saying of Mani where he claimed that when light saw darkness had struck it and mixed with it, it brought about this world to thereby free the parts of light from the parts of darkness. So the world, on this saying, after the temporal mixture, becomes something brought into being after a temporal thing [yet] ancient. And that is ignorance. So they necessitated the incapacity of light at the time of its being in its dominion with all its helpers of good things and supporters of beauties, when it could not prevent the striking of darkness and one of its factions from it, and they made it ignorant of the time of striking into it to free itself. Then they claimed that it brought about this world to free its parts from it after it had become captive in its bonds— far, far are they from that! And how ignorant is he whom they put forward and make him every and the first of every good and knowledge, while he was ignorant of what was mentioned, and magnified every good with power, yet he was powerless.
Source pages p. 633

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of the dualist (Manichaean) doctrine that the world originates from the mixture of two eternal principles: light (good) and darkness (evil). The passage begins by discussing a misconception (tawahhum) about temporal events and eternity, then transitions to the dualist claim. Maturidi argues that if the two principles were originally separate and then mixed, the mixture itself is a temporal event (hadith), so the world arising from that mixture cannot be eternal. He addresses a possible objection: that light and darkness were always in their respective places but later mixed. This, he says, invalidates the claim of an eternal mixed world. He then critiques Mani's specific claim that light, upon being attacked by darkness, created the world to free its parts. Maturidi points out the logical absurdities: it implies that light was powerless when in its own dominion, unable to prevent the attack, and ignorant of the timing; moreover, it suggests light needed to create a world to liberate itself, but then the world would be contingent on a temporal event, undermining its eternity. The passage ends with a rhetorical condemnation of such ignorance.

Key Terms
الظلة
النور
الامتزاج
الماني
قدم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The dualist belief that two eternal principles mixing can produce an eternal world.
Correction: Maturidi shows that the mixture itself is temporal, so the resulting world cannot be eternal.

Checkpoint
According to Maturidi, why cannot the world be eternal if it arises from the mixture of light and darkness?
A) Because light and darkness are not eternal
B) Because the mixture itself is a temporal event
C) Because darkness is evil
D) Because light cannot mix with darkness

Key Takeaway
The mixture of two eternal principles is inherently temporal, so any world resulting from it cannot be eternal.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If the dualists were able to think through their own logic and see that they were attributing ignorance and impotence to the light, would your own heart be willing to examine where you might be inadvertently limiting God's power or knowledge in your life? What one area will you actively check today?
?
When you face a situation where evil seems to triumph, do you ever feel that God is unable to act or unaware of your struggle? How does Maturidi's refutation of dualism call you to repent of such thoughts and reaffirm God's absolute control and wisdom?
?
The dualists claimed light created the world to fix a problem it could not prevent. Are there any beliefs you hold about God that subtly imply He needs creation to complete His purpose or remedy a flaw? Will you let this passage strip away such ideas and trust that God is perfect and self-sufficient?

Refutation of Dualist Eternity by Mixture
Maturidi refutes dualist claim that world is eternal through mixture of light and darkness.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's rational refutation of the dualist claim that the world is eternal through mixture of light and darkness.

Core Idea
Mixture is an event; what is originated cannot be eternal.

Maturidi confronts the dualist worldview that posits two eternal principles—light and darkness—whose mixture produces the world. He argues that mixture itself is an originated event, so the resulting world cannot be eternal. The passage then exposes the absurdities of the Manichaean version of this myth.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and identify the claim
Read the Arabic and translation. Notice the dualists claim light and darkness were distinct then mixed, resulting in the world. The key term is 'امتزاج' (mixture).
2
Follow Maturidi's core argument
Maturidi argues that mixture is an originated event (حادث), so the world cannot be eternal. The analogy of the elder shows the fallacy of projecting a temporal attribute onto the eternal.
3
Analyze the Manichaean version and its contradictions
Maturidi exposes how Mani's story implies light's incapacity and ignorance, and that the world being mostly evil contradicts light's goodness. This leads to absurdities like light aiding darkness.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وما ذكر من التوهم فإنه قد يتوهم في كل شيخ - في أول ما شاخ بقدميه، ولم يجب به الوصف في الأزل به الوصف في الأزل، وكذا في كل حركة وسكون، وتفرق واجتماع. فإن قلت: ذا محال، فمثله كون الحدث في الأزل محال، والله الموفق: ازعم من يقول بالإثنين - الظلة والنور - بقدم العالم، وأحق من كانا يأبى ذلك من بقول بهذا؛ إذ من قولهم إنهما كانا متباينين فامتزجا، فكان العالم من امتزاجها. ومعلوم أن الإمتزاج كان حادثاً، إذ التباين كان هو المتقدم ولم يكونا يلقان بالعالم. إلا أن يقولوا: النور والظلمة جوهران اختلفا، في الأصل بمكانهما، فكان مكان النور نور كله وخير، ومكان الظلمة ظلمة كلها وشر، فيبطل القول بقدم العالم الممتزج، وبخاصة قول الماني حيث زعم أن النور لما رأى الظلمة قدحت فيه ومازجت به أحدث هذا العالم؛ ليتخلص بذلك أجزاء النور من أجزاء الظلمة، فصار العالم على هذا القول بعد الامتزاج المحدث مكون بعد المحدث قديما. وذلك هو التجاهل. فأوجبوا عجز النور وقت كونه في سلطانه بجميع أعوانه من الخيرات وأنصاره من الحسنات حيث لم يقدر على الإمتناع من قدح الظلمة وأحد أحزابه عنه، وجهلوه بوقت القدح فيه ليتخلص منه، ثم زعموا أنه أحدث هذا العالم ليخلص أجزاءه منه بعد أن هيهات ما أبعدهم عن ذلك، وما أجهل من يقدمونه ويجعلون له كل أول كل خير وعلم، وقد جهل ما ذكر، وعظم كل خير بقوة، وقد عجز. ثم إذ كان هو المنشئ للعالم كيف صار أكثر العالم شرا؟ فهو إذن فعل الشر ليتخلص به من وثاق الشر، فكأنه أعان الشر والظلمة؛ إذ هو عمل ذلك. ثم قد زاد من أجزائه في أجزاء النور بإحداث العالم في أجزاء العالم، فازداد له حبسا وهلاكا، ولا قوة إلا بالله
And what was mentioned of illusion, for one may be illusioned about every elder - at the first time he grew old with his two feet, and the attribute in pre-eternity did not necessitate the attribute in pre-eternity, and likewise in every motion and stillness, separation and union. If you say: this is impossible, then similarly the occurrence of an event in pre-eternity is impossible, and God is the giver of success: I take refuge from those who say two - darkness and light - with the eternity of the world, and the most deserving of them both reject that from those who say this; for it is from their statement that they were distinct then mixed, so the world came from their mixture. And it is known that the mixture was originated, since distinctness was prior and they did not give rise to the world. Unless they say: light and darkness are two substances that differed originally in their places, so the place of light was all light and good, and the place of darkness was all darkness and evil, then the statement about the eternity of the mixed world is nullified, especially the statement of Mani who claimed that when light saw that darkness had struck it and mixed with it, it brought about this world in order to liberate thereby the parts of light from the parts of darkness, so the world according to this statement after the originated mixture became a created thing after the originated [mixture] eternal. That is ignorance. So they necessitated the incapacity of light at the time of its being in its dominion with all its helpers of good things and its supporters of good deeds, for it was not able to prevent the striking of darkness and one of its parties from it, and they were ignorant of it at the time of the strike in order to be liberated from it, then they claimed that it brought about this world to liberate its parts from it after that. Alas! How far they are from that, and how ignorant is the one they elevate and make for him every first of every good and knowledge, while he has been ignorant of what was mentioned, and he magnified every good with power, and he was incapable. Then since he was the originator of the world, how did most of the world become evil? So he then did evil to liberate himself from the captivity of evil, as if he aided evil and darkness, since he did that. Then he increased from his parts in the parts of light by bringing about the world in the parts of the world, so he increased for himself imprisonment and destruction. And there is no power except with God.
Source pages p. 633

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of the dualist (الثنوية) claim that the world is eternal because it results from the mixture (الإمتزاج) of two eternal principles: light (النور) and darkness (الظلمة). Maturidi begins by drawing an analogy: just as one may be illusioned about an elder—the attribute of elderhood does not necessitate that attribute from pre-eternity—so the dualists are deluded in thinking mixture can be eternal. The key argument: mixture is an event (حادث), so it cannot be eternal. The dualists say light and darkness were distinct and then mixed. Since mixture is originated, the world cannot be eternal. Maturidi then refutes the Manichaean (المانوي) version: Mani claimed that light saw darkness striking it and mixed, so light created the world to liberate its particles. This implies that light was incapable of preventing the strike despite having all helpers, and ignorant of the timing—contradicting its divinity. Furthermore, if light is the originator of the world, why is most of the world evil? This would mean light did evil to free itself, aiding darkness, and actually increased its imprisonment. Thus the dualist account is self-contradictory and absurd.

Key Terms
النور
الظلمة
الإمتزاج
الثنوية
المانوية
قدم العالم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Dualism (two eternal principles) is a rational alternative to monotheism.
Correction: Maturidi shows dualism leads to logical contradictions: mixture is an event, light cannot be omnipotent/omniscient, and the world's evil contradicts light's goodness.

Checkpoint
According to Maturidi, what is the main reason the world cannot be eternal in the dualist view?
A) Light and darkness are both good
B) Mixture is an originated event
C) The world is full of evil
D) Light is stronger than darkness

Key Takeaway
Maturidi refutes dualism by showing that mixture is an event, and the Manichaean version implies light's impotence and the world's evil, contradicting its divinity.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you see evil in the world, do you ever doubt that God is all-powerful and all-good? How does Maturidi’s refutation of the dualist’s claim that light was incapable challenge that doubt?
?
The dualist believed that light could not prevent darkness from striking. In what areas of your life are you tempted to believe that God cannot overcome the darkness you face? What would it mean to truly rely on His power?
?
Mani claimed that light created the world to liberate its parts, yet this only increased imprisonment. Have you ever taken an action that you thought would free you from a problem, but it actually made things worse? How does this lesson warn you about trusting your own plans rather than God’s wisdom?

Dualist Confusion on Mixture and the Imperfection of Light and Darkness
Maturidi exposes dualist confusion on mixture and the mutable nature of light and darkness.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Maturidi refutes dualism by pointing out the dualists' internal disagreement about mixture and by demonstrating that light and darkness possess mutable and imperfect qualities that disqualify them from divinity.

Core Idea
Light and darkness are not divine because they change, mix, and contain both good and evil.

Maturidi continues his refutation of dualism by first noting that dualists themselves disagree on how mixture occurs—some attribute it to darkness, others to light. He then establishes a general principle: both light and darkness are subject to change, transformation, and contain opposites, which directly contradicts the attributes of a divine being. This lesson unpacks that argument.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on dualist disagreement
Read the Arabic passage and translation carefully, noting the different dualist views on mixture.
Maturidi dismisses these views as 'hadian' (nonsense).
2
Identify the shared properties of light and darkness
Find the list of properties: they are subject to change, transformation, partition, good/evil, etc.
These properties show they are not perfect.
3
Understand the conclusion of non-divinity
Because they are mutable and part of the world, they cannot be divine. Their inability to produce an act proves they are effects.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال أبو منصور رحمه الله: واختلفت الثنوية في الإمتزاج، فمنهم من يجعله ومنهم من يأبى ذلك ويراه للظلمة، لكنهم اختلفوا، فمنهم من يحقق له الفعل كالمنتشر بالطبع، وهي كثيفة ستارة، والنور رقيق دراك فيقع فيها، الإمتزاج بذلك. ومنهم من يجعل ذلك للنور فوقع لكنه كله هذيان، ما يدريهم ذلك؟ والأصل فيه أن الظلمة والنور في احتمال التغير والاستحالة، واحتمال التجزئة والتبعيض والحسن والقبح، والطيب والخبيث، وكل شيء سواء، فإن كانا يرجعان إلى أجزاء العالم فها يحدثان يحدثه ويفنيان بفنائه. ثم لا يجوز أن يكون لواحد منها ألوهيته؛ لظهور هو دليل قوي عليم حكيم، فهما في تلك الجملة العجز والجهل بهما، والعالم و بعد، إذ لم يكن واحد منها قدر أن ينشئ فعلا يدل عليه ثبت أنهما مفعولان لا فاعلان. ومما يبين أنهما فعل الواحد، ما ليس في العالم شيء بجوهره حتى لا يكون منه شر في وجه أبدا، ولا شر لا يكون منه خير في وجه أبدا، ثبت أن إنكار مثله عن الواحد غير ممكن. ثم الأصل أن الإمتزاج لا يخلو من أن يكون شرا أو خيرا، فإن كان خيرا من الظلمة، فيكون منها الخير، ويبطل قولهم بالاثنين من أن يكون من حيث لا يكون من الشر خير ولا من الخير شر، وإن كان شرا فقد شاركه الخير في القبول فصار شرا، وإن كان ذلك من النور فالوجهان قائمان فيه. ما إذ كانا غير ممتزجين فامتزجا، لا يخلو امتزاجها من أن يكون بانفسها فيكونان ممتزجين بالجوهر متباينين به، وذلك متناقض. ولو جاز ذلك لجاز أن .
Abu Mansur (may God have mercy on him) said: The dualists differed concerning mixture; some of them attribute it [to something] and some reject that and consider it [as belonging] to darkness, but they differed. Among them are those who actualize the action for it like the spreading by nature, and it is dense and veiling, and light is subtle and penetrating, so it falls into it, and mixture happens by that. And among them are those who make that for light, so mixture occurred, but all of it is nonsense, what do they know of that? The basis in it is that darkness and light are subject to possibility of change and transformation, and possibility of partition and division, and good and evil, and pleasant and repugnant, and everything equal. If they both trace back to parts of the world then they produce what it produces and perish with its perishing. Then it is not permissible that divinity belong to either of them; because there appears a strong, knowing, wise proof, so they are in that totality of incapacity and ignorance concerning them, and the world, and after, since neither of them was able to produce an act indicating it, it is established that they are effects (مفعولان) not agents (فاعلان). And among what shows that they are the act of the One is that there is nothing in the world by its essence such that no evil comes from it in any way, and no evil such that no good comes from it in any way, it is established that denying the like of this concerning the One is not possible. Then the principle is that mixture is not free from being either evil or good. If it is good from darkness, then good comes from it, and their statement of two is nullified because good does not come from evil nor evil from good. And if it is evil, then good has shared in acceptance, so it becomes evil. And if that is from light, then both faces are established in it. Now since they were not mixed and then mixed, their mixture is not free from being from themselves, so they would be mixed in essence and distinct in it, and that is contradictory. And if that were permissible, it would be permissible that ...
Source pages p. 634

Commentary
This passage continues the refutation of dualism by first noting variations among dualists: some attribute the mixture to darkness, others to light. Maturidi dismisses these as nonsense. He then presents a general proof that light and darkness cannot be divine: they are subject to change, transformation, partition, and contain both good and evil—unlike a divine being. Since they are mutable and compound, they are part of the world and thus originated. Moreover, neither can produce an independent act, proving they are created effects (مفعولان) rather than agents (فاعلان). The argument from mixture: if mixture is good coming from darkness, that contradicts the dualist premise that darkness is purely evil; if it is evil, then good participated, making it mixed. Finally, the logical contradiction: if two distinct things become mixed, either they mix by themselves (which would mean they are simultaneously mixed in essence and distinct, a contradiction), or they are mixed by an external agent—which would be the single creator. Thus dualism fails.

Key Terms
الثنوية
الإمتزاج
التغير
الاستحالة
المفعول
الفاعل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think that light and darkness, being fundamental principles, are beyond change and imperfection.
Correction: Maturidi shows they are subject to change, transformation, and contain opposites, directly contradicting divine perfection.

Checkpoint
What does Maturidi conclude about light and darkness based on their inability to produce an act?
A) They are eternal agents
B) They are created effects
C) They are partially divine
D) They are beyond good and evil

Key Takeaway
Light and darkness, being mutable, imperfect, and unable to independently act, cannot be divine—they are created effects.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you examine your heart, what 'created lights' (talents, successes, relationships) do you secretly treat as permanent and reliable, forgetting they are subject to change? How does Maturidi's argument expose that idolatry?
?
The dualists blamed darkness for evil and considered themselves 'light.' Where in your life do you divide the world into pure good (you) and pure evil (others) instead of acknowledging that all creation contains a mixture? Are you willing to repent of that self-righteousness today?
?
If neither light nor darkness can produce an independent act, then every good thought or deed you have is a gift from the true Agent. Will you consciously attribute your next good action to Him alone, or will you claim it as your own?

Sects and Disagreements

The Logical Impossibility of Mixture and Proof of One Creator
Maturidi argues that mixture contradictions and the mix of good and evil prove a single creator.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's arguments that mixture between light and darkness leads to contradictions, and that the world's mixture of good and evil points to a single creator.

Core Idea
Mixture creates contradictions, and the coexistence of good and evil proves one creator.

In the second part of his refutation, Maturidi presents further arguments: the fact that nothing in the world is purely good or purely evil suggests a single source. Moreover, the very concept of mixture between two distinct substances leads to logical contradictions. This lesson explores these powerful arguments.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on the argument from mixture
Read the Arabic passage and translation, focusing on the analysis of mixture being good or evil.
Notice the premise: nothing in the world is purely good or purely evil.
2
Analyze the implications of mixture quality
If mixture is good from darkness, then good comes from evil, contradicting dualism. If evil, then good participated.
Either way, dualism's fundamental separation fails.
3
Understand the logical contradiction of self-mixture
If light and darkness mix by themselves, they would be both mixed in essence and distinct, which is impossible.
Thus, mixture requires an external agent—the one Creator.

قال أبو منصور رحمه الله: واختلفت الثنوية في الإمتزاج، فمنهم من يجعله ومنهم من يأبى ذلك ويراه للظلمة، لكنهم اختلفوا، فمنهم من يحقق له الفعل كالمنتشر بالطبع، وهي كثيفة ستارة، والنور رقيق دراك فيقع فيها، الإمتزاج بذلك. ومنهم من يجعل ذلك للنور فوقع لكنه كله هذيان، ما يدريهم ذلك؟ والأصل فيه أن الظلمة والنور في احتمال التغير والاستحالة، واحتمال التجزئة والتبعيض والحسن والقبح، والطيب والخبيث، وكل شيء سواء، فإن كانا يرجعان إلى أجزاء العالم فها يحدثان يحدثه ويفنيان بفنائه. ثم لا يجوز أن يكون لواحد منها ألوهيته؛ لظهور هو دليل قوي عليم حكيم، فهما في تلك الجملة العجز والجهل بهما، والعالم و بعد، إذ لم يكن واحد منها قدر أن ينشئ فعلا يدل عليه ثبت أنهما مفعولان لا فاعلان. ومما يبين أنهما فعل الواحد، ما ليس في العالم شيء بجوهره حتى لا يكون منه شر في وجه أبدا، ولا شر لا يكون منه خير في وجه أبدا، ثبت أن إنكار مثله عن الواحد غير ممكن. ثم الأصل أن الإمتزاج لا يخلو من أن يكون شرا أو خيرا، فإن كان خيرا من الظلمة، فيكون منها الخير، ويبطل قولهم بالاثنين من أن يكون من حيث لا يكون من الشر خير ولا من الخير شر، وإن كان شرا فقد شاركه الخير في القبول فصار شرا، وإن كان ذلك من النور فالوجهان قائمان فيه. ما إذ كانا غير ممتزجين فامتزجا، لا يخلو امتزاجها من أن يكون بانفسها فيكونان ممتزجين بالجوهر متباينين به، وذلك متناقض. ولو جاز ذلك لجاز أن .
Abu Mansur (may God have mercy on him) said: The dualists differed concerning mixture; some of them attribute it [to something] and some reject that and consider it [as belonging] to darkness, but they differed. Among them are those who actualize the action for it like the spreading by nature, and it is dense and veiling, and light is subtle and penetrating, so it falls into it, and mixture happens by that. And among them are those who make that for light, so mixture occurred, but all of it is nonsense, what do they know of that? The basis in it is that darkness and light are subject to possibility of change and transformation, and possibility of partition and division, and good and evil, and pleasant and repugnant, and everything equal. If they both trace back to parts of the world then they produce what it produces and perish with its perishing. Then it is not permissible that divinity belong to either of them; because there appears a strong, knowing, wise proof, so they are in that totality of incapacity and ignorance concerning them, and the world, and after, since neither of them was able to produce an act indicating it, it is established that they are effects (مفعولان) not agents (فاعلان). And among what shows that they are the act of the One is that there is nothing in the world by its essence such that no evil comes from it in any way, and no evil such that no good comes from it in any way, it is established that denying the like of this concerning the One is not possible. Then the principle is that mixture is not free from being either evil or good. If it is good from darkness, then good comes from it, and their statement of two is nullified because good does not come from evil nor evil from good. And if it is evil, then good has shared in acceptance, so it becomes evil. And if that is from light, then both faces are established in it. Now since they were not mixed and then mixed, their mixture is not free from being from themselves, so they would be mixed in essence and distinct in it, and that is contradictory. And if that were permissible, it would be permissible that ...
Source pages p. 634

Commentary
This passage continues the refutation of dualism by first noting variations among dualists: some attribute the mixture to darkness, others to light. Maturidi dismisses these as nonsense. He then presents a general proof that light and darkness cannot be divine: they are subject to change, transformation, partition, and contain both good and evil—unlike a divine being. Since they are mutable and compound, they are part of the world and thus originated. Moreover, neither can produce an independent act, proving they are created effects (مفعولان) rather than agents (فاعلان). The argument from mixture: if mixture is good coming from darkness, that contradicts the dualist premise that darkness is purely evil; if it is evil, then good participated, making it mixed. Finally, the logical contradiction: if two distinct things become mixed, either they mix by themselves (which would mean they are simultaneously mixed in essence and distinct, a contradiction), or they are mixed by an external agent—which would be the single creator. Thus dualism fails.

Correction: Maturidi shows that mixture leads to logical contradictions and actually proves a single creator.

This lesson demands humility: can you look at someone you consider evil and still acknowledge the good in them, as a sign of the single Creator's work?

Refuting the Dualist Theory of Mixing
Maturidi refutes dualist views on mixing and demonstrates the createdness of light and darkness.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's refutation of the dualist claim that light and darkness are eternal principles that mix to create the world.

Core Idea
Both light and darkness are created, not eternal; their changes prove they cannot be gods.

Maturidi confronts the dualist claim that light and darkness are eternal principles that mix to form the world. He points out their internal disagreements and then argues that both are subject to change, division, and good/evil—characteristics that prove they are created and not divine. This lesson walks through his refutation.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic carefully, then the English translation to grasp Maturidi's structure.
Notice the internal disagreements of the dualists.
2
Identify the key arguments
Look for the points: both are subject to change, cannot be eternal, cannot produce action, and nothing is purely good/evil.
3
Reflect on the conclusion
Understand why Maturidi concludes that both are 'maf'ulan' not 'fa'ilan' and why denial of the One is impossible.
Connect to tawhid.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال أبو منصور رحمه الله : واختلفت الثنوية فى الإمتزاج، فمنهم من يجعله ومنهم من يأبى ذلك ويراه للظلمة، لكنهم اختلفوا، فمنهم من يحقق له الفعل كالمنتشر بالطبع، وهي كثيفة ستارة، والنور رقيق دراك فيقع فيها، الإمتزاج بذلك. ومنهم من يجعل ذلك للنور. فوقع لكنه كله هذيان، ما يدريهم ذلك؟ والأصل فيه أن الظلمة والنور في احتمال التغير والاستحالة، واحتمال التجزئة والتبعيض والحسن والقبح، والطيب والخبيث، وكل شيء سواء، فإن كانا يرجعان إلى أجزاء العالم فها يحدثان يحدثه ويفنيان بفنائه. ثم لا يجوز أن يكون لواحد منها ألوهيته؛ لظهور هو دليل قوي عليم حكيم، فهما في تلك الجملة العجز والجهل بهما، والعالم بعد، إذ لم يكن واحد منها قدر أن ينشئ فعلا يدل عليه ثبت أنهما مفعولان لا فاعلان. ومما يبين أنهما فعل الواحد، ما ليس في العالم شيء بجوهره حتى لا يكون منه شر في وجه أبدا، ولا شر لا يكون منه خير في وجه أبدا، ثبت أن إنكار مثله عن الواحد غير ممكن.
Abu Mansur (may Allah have mercy on him) said: The dualists differed regarding mixing: among them are those who affirm it and among them are those who reject it and consider it for darkness, but they differed. Among them are those who attribute the action to it as spreading by nature, and it (darkness) is thick covering, while light is subtle and grasping, so it falls into it; mixing is by that. And among them are those who attribute that to light. So it occurred, but it is all nonsense; what makes them know that? The principle in it is that darkness and light are subject to change and transformation, and subject to division and partitioning, and good and evil, and pleasant and unpleasant, and everything is equal. If they both refer to the parts of the world, then they come to be and pass away with its passing. Then it is not permissible that divinity belong to either of them, because there appears a proof of a powerful, knowing, wise (creator). So they both, in that totality, are characterized by incapacity and ignorance, and the world is after, since neither of them was able to produce an action indicating upon it, it is established that they are both acted upon (maf'ulan) not actors (fa'ilan). And among what makes clear that they are the act of the One is that there is nothing in the world that is purely by its essence such that no evil comes from it ever, nor evil from which no good comes ever. It is established that denial of the like of this from the One is not possible.
Source pages p. 634

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of the dualist claim that there are two eternal principles, light and darkness, and that they mix. Maturidi begins by noting that the dualists themselves disagree about mixing: some affirm it, some deny it, and among those who affirm, some attribute the action of mixing to darkness, others to light. Maturidi dismisses this as nonsense, because both light and darkness are subject to change, transformation, division, and qualities like good and evil. This shows they are not eternal or divine. If they were parts of the world, they would be subject to generation and corruption. Therefore, neither can be a god. The existence of a single all-powerful, all-knowing creator is evident. Since neither light nor darkness can produce any action that indicates divinity, they must both be created (maf'ulan) rather than creators (fa'ilan). Moreover, nothing in the world is purely good without any evil, nor purely evil without any good. This shows that the creator, being one, encompasses both aspects. Hence, the denial of the unified creator is impossible.

Correction: He refutes it by showing they are subject to change and thus created, not divine.

Key Takeaway
Maturidi refutes dualism by demonstrating that light and darkness are created, as evidenced by their mutability, inability to act, and the absence of pure good or evil in the world.

Today, commit to saying 'Qadr Allah' when hardship strikes, recognizing that there is no co-creator.
?

If not, will you ask Allah to show you the wisdom behind it, trusting that He alone creates both good and evil for a purpose?
?
Light and darkness are subject to change, yet we often cling to temporary things as if they were permanent. What is something in your life—a possession, a relationship, a feeling—that you treat as if it were eternal? How does realizing its createdness and mutability affect your attachment to it? Will you let go and turn to the only unchanging One?

Maturidi's Dilemma Against the Dualists
Maturidi argues that mixing must be either good or evil, both cases contradict dualist claims.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's logical argument that mixing necessarily leads to a contradiction in the dualist claim of strict separation between good and evil.

Core Idea
Mixing forces dualists to admit good from evil or evil from good—contradicting their own principle.

Maturidi presents a clever logical dilemma targeting the dualist doctrine of two eternal principles (light and darkness) from which only good or only evil can arise. By focusing on the concept of mixing (imtizaj), he shows that any possible case undermines their foundational claim.

Guided Path
1
Understand the dualist claim
Remind yourself that dualists hold that light produces only good, darkness produces only evil, and never do good come from darkness or evil from light.
2
Read the passage
Read the Arabic passage once, then its translation. Notice how Maturidi sets up the dilemma using the concept of mixing.
Pay attention to the two branches: mixing is either good or evil.
3
Follow the dilemma branches
Work through each case: if mixing is good, it comes from darkness (contradiction); if mixing is evil, good participates in it (contradiction). See how both lead to inconsistency.
Also note the final statement: if mixing is from light, both branches apply.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم الأصل أن الإمتزاج لا يخلو من أن يكون شرا أو خيرا، فإن كان خيرا من الظلمة، فيكون منها الخير، ويطل قولهم بالاثنين من أن يكون من حيث لا يكون من الشر خير ولا من الخير شر، وإن كان شرا فقد شاركه الخير فى القبول فصار شرا، وإن كان ذلك من النور فالوجهان قائمان فيه
Then the principle is that mixing must be either evil or good. If it is good from darkness, then good comes from it, and their statement about twoness is invalidated, in that no good comes from evil and no evil from good. And if it is evil, then good has shared in acceptance and it becomes evil. And if that (mixing) is from light, then both aspects apply to it.
Source pages p. 634

Commentary
This passage presents a logical dilemma for the dualists. They claim that darkness is the source of all evil and light is the source of all good, and that never does good come from darkness or evil from light. Maturidi forces them to consider mixing: if mixing is good and it comes from darkness, then good comes from darkness, contradicting their claim. If mixing is evil, then good (light) participates in it, meaning light is involved in evil, again contradicting their separation. If mixing is from light, then the same two possibilities apply. Thus, their position is self-contradictory.

اثنين

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think the argument is about the value of mixing, but it is about undermining the dualist separation of good and evil.
Correction: Maturidi is not interested in whether mixing is good or bad per se; rather he uses the classification to force a contradiction in the dualist system.

Checkpoint

A) It proves the existence of two gods.
B) It is logically consistent.
C) It leads to contradiction.
D) It is impossible to know.

Key Takeaway

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

But what if, as Maturidi argues, they are mixed? How would that change the way you own your actions? ?
When you witness evil arising from something you thought was purely good—say, a religious practice that leads to arrogance—do you deny the connection? Maturidi forces you to admit that good can produce evil if it is mixed. Can you examine your own practices and admit where good intentions have yielded bad outcomes? Will you repent of the evil that came from your own good?
?
The dualist in you wants to keep your good deeds pure and blame your bad deeds on external forces. But this argument shows that such separation is impossible. What one excuse are you currently hiding behind? 'I was raised that way' or 'my environment made me'? Today, will you accept full responsibility for all the 'mixing' in your soul and ask Allah to purify it, knowing that only He is the source of pure good?

Maturidi's Proof of Origination through Impossibility of Self-Mixing
Maturidi proves that mixing cannot be by themselves, proving they are originated.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's logical argument that the mixing of two distinct entities cannot be by themselves, and how this proves their originatedness.

Core Idea
If mixing were self-caused, light and darkness would be contradictions; therefore, they require an external cause—proving their origination.

Imam Maturidi presents a logical proof that light and darkness cannot be eternal. He examines the very act of their mixing: if they could mix by themselves, they would have to be both mixed and distinct simultaneously—a contradiction. This impossibility leads to the conclusion that something outside them caused their mixing and distinctness, which means they are originated, not eternal.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the English translation. Notice the conditional structure: 'If they mix by themselves, then...'
The phrase 'ما يفسد' is uncertain in Arabic; rely on the translation for meaning.
2
Identify the core contradiction
Maturidi argues that if mixing were self-caused, the entities would be simultaneously mixed and distinct in essence. Understand why this is logically impossible.
Contradictory attributes cannot coexist in the same subject at the same time.
3
Follow the reductio ad absurdum
He extends the argument: if self-caused change were possible, then contradictory states (moving/stationary, alive/dead) could also coexist. Grasp why this is absurd.
This strengthens the proof by showing the consequences of accepting self-causation.
4
Connect to the conclusion
He then states that all state changes require an external cause (ghayr). Apply this to mixing and distinctness, leading to the conclusion that the entities are originated (hadith).
Origination here means they came into existence after not existing.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ما إذ كانا غير ممتزجين فامتزجا، لا يخلو امتزاجها من أن يكون بانفسها فيكونان ممتزجين بالجوهر متباينين به، وذلك متناقض. ولو جاز ذلك لجاز أن يكونا متحركين بأنفسهما ساكنين حيين ميتين قاعدين قائمين ما يفسد مع أن يكون التباين لنفس يقع، ثم امتزاج بما كان به التباين. ألا ترى أن الأحوال تتغير بالأعيان لم يجز وجودها إلا بغير، فكذلك التباين والإمتزاج، فثبت أن ما بغير همنا امتزجا وبغيرهما كانا متباينين وذلك يوجب حدثهما.
When they were not mixed and then mixed, their mixing cannot be by themselves, for then they would be mixed in essence and distinct in essence, and that is contradictory. And if that were permissible, it would be permissible for them to be moving by themselves while stationary, living while dead, sitting while standing, which spoils [the argument], along with the fact that distinctness is by essence, then mixing by what distinguished them? Do you not see that states change by entities; they cannot exist except by another. So likewise distinctness and mixing. So it is established that they mixed by something other than them and were distinct by something other than them, and that necessitates their origination (huduth).
Source pages p. 634

Commentary
This passage is a proof that light and darkness cannot be eternal. Maturidi argues: before mixing, they were distinct; after mixing, they are mixed. If the mixing were by themselves, they would have to be simultaneously mixed and distinct in essence, a logical contradiction. If such a self-caused change were possible, then all sorts of contradictory states could coexist, like being moving and still, alive and dead. But this is absurd. Changes in states (like from distinct to mixed) require an external cause. Therefore, their mixing and distinctness are caused by something other than them, proving that they are originated (hadith). If they were eternal, they would not be subject to such changes.

Key Terms
الإمتزاج
التباين
الحدوث
الذات
الغير

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Maturidi is attacking the concept of mixing itself or discussing physical properties of light and darkness.
Correction: He is using mixing as a logical example to prove that the entities cannot be eternal; the proof is metaphysical, not physical.

Checkpoint
How does Maturidi prove that light and darkness are originated?
A) By showing they are subject to heat.
B) By arguing that their mixing cannot be by themselves and requires an external cause.
C) By quoting scripture.
D) By observing their brightness.

Key Takeaway
Maturidi demonstrates that the mixing and distinctness of light and darkness require an external cause, proving they are originated, not eternal.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
In what areas of your life do you act as if you are self-sufficient, believing you can change your own heart or habits without external help? How does Maturidi's proof that even mixing requires an outside cause expose the fallacy of such self-reliance?
?
When you examine your own changes—your moods, beliefs, actions—do you honestly attribute them to yourself or to the One who causes all change? What would change in your daily life if you lived with the constant awareness that every state you experience is caused by another?
?
If Maturidi's logic convinces you that light and darkness cannot mix by themselves, how can you claim control over even the smallest detail of your life? What step will you take today to consciously acknowledge your complete dependence on the Creator?

Contradictions in the Dualists' View on Slaughter and Knowledge
Maturidi highlights contradictions in dualist views on slaughter and the impossibility of their pursuit of wisdom.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Maturidi exposes contradictions in dualist principles regarding slaughter and the pursuit of wisdom.

Core Idea
Dualists cannot logically permit slaughter or seek knowledge without contradicting their own tenets.

The dualists posit two eternal principles: light (good) and darkness (evil). Maturidi shows that their own rules lead to contradictions: they forbid slaughter but their principles require it, and their view of knowledge makes learning impossible. This passage exposes the internal inconsistency of their worldview.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage and its literal translation to get the flow of Maturidi's argument.
The passage is dense and slightly incomplete; focus on the logical steps.
2
Analyze the contradiction on slaughter
Notice how Maturidi points out that dualists forbid slaughter, yet their own description of light and darkness implies that slaughter separates good from evil and should be permitted.
This shows their first inconsistency.
3
Examine the impossibility of knowledge
Maturidi argues that if ignorance is evil, and light never produces evil, then dualists cannot be ignorant from light; if from darkness, they cannot learn because darkness does not accept good. Thus their quest for wisdom is self-defeating.
This undermines their entire intellectual project.

ثم وبعد، فإنهم يقولون بحرمة الذبائح، وأحق من يحل هم؛ إذ بها التفريق بين الجسد المظلم وبين الروح المضئ، وبين النور الجلى والظلمة الستارة، ذلك وصفوا النور بأنه رقيق دراك، وبالروح ذلك لا بالظلمة، فيجب حل الذبح، ولا قوة إلا بالله وأصله أنهم ينكرون الشر من جوهر الخير، والخير من جوهر الشر، هذا الذي حملهم على القول باثنين قد أثبتوا الإقرار بالقتل، وبما هو عندهم معصية، فلو كان من غير الذي منه القتل فقد كذب، وهو شر، ولو كان منه فقد صدق بالاقرار بالمعصية، ثون أن العجز عن إدراك المحكمة فى خلق الشر لا يضطر إلى القول باثنين. فيه تحقيقه أيضا، ولا قوة إلا بالله . على أنهم أحق الخلق في الإمتناع عن النطق بالحكمة أو طلب العلم لأن قولهم : إن جوهر النور لا يجى منه شر قط. والجهل شر، فإن كان من ذلك الجوهر فهو عالم جوهرة حكيم به، لا يحتمل الجهل ولا السفه، والتعلم وطلب الحكمة حق الجهال بهما، وإن كان من جوهر الشر فإنه لا ينجع فيه؛ لأنه بجوهره لا يقبل ولا يحتمل الخير. وإذا كان كذلك بطلت مناظرتهم ودعواهم والعالم؛ لأن مناظرتهم في ذلك لو كانت جوهر النور كان هو عالماً جوهر الظلمة كان غير قابل ولا قبل المناظرة فلا معنى لها، ولو كانت مع
Then after that, they say about the prohibition of sacrifices, and they are most entitled to permit them, because by it there is separation between the dark body and the luminous spirit, and between the clear light and the covering darkness. That is, they described light as subtle and grasping, and the spirit is like that, not darkness. So slaughter must be permitted. And there is no power except with Allah. And the origin is that they deny evil from the essence of good, and good from the essence of evil. This is what led them to say there are two. They have affirmed confession of murder, and what according to them is disobedience. So if it was from other than that from which murder came, then he lied, and it is evil. And if it was from it, then he spoke truth by confessing disobedience. Then that inability to comprehend the wisdom in the creation of evil does not compel the belief in two. In it is also its verification. And there is no power except with Allah. Moreover, they are the most deserving of creation to refrain from speaking wisdom or seeking knowledge, because their statement: 'The essence of light never brings any evil.' And ignorance is evil. If it is from that essence, then he is knowing, wise essence, not susceptible to ignorance or foolishness. And learning and seeking wisdom is the right of the ignorant of them. And if it is from the essence of evil, then it is not effective in it, because by its essence it does not accept nor admit good. And if that is so, their debate and their claim are invalid, and the world? Because their debate in that: if it were the essence of light, then he is knowing; the essence of darkness is not receptive, and does not accept debate, so there is no meaning to it. And if it were
Source pages p. 635

Commentary
This passage addresses the dualists' practical contradictions and the impossibility of their pursuit of wisdom. Maturidi points out that the dualists claim to prohibit sacrifices, but they are the ones who should permit them because they believe that slaughter separates the dark body from the luminous spirit. Their own description of light as subtle and grasping implies that the soul is light, so slaughter separates the good (light) from the evil (darkness). Thus, they contradict themselves by forbidding what their own principles entail. Furthermore, they deny that good can come from evil or evil from good, yet they acknowledge that confession of murder is disobedience. If the confession comes from a different source than the murder, then it is a lie, which is evil; if from the same source, then it is true but involves evil. Thus, they are inconsistent. Maturidi then argues that their inability to understand the wisdom in the creation of evil does not justify believing in two principles. Finally, he points out that their own statements make the pursuit of knowledge futile: if ignorance is evil and the essence of light never produces evil, then they cannot be ignorant; if they are ignorant, that evil must come from the darkness, but then they cannot learn because darkness does not accept good. So their own system invalidates any discussion or debate.

Key Terms

الجسد المظلم
الروح المضئ
جوهر النور
جوهر الظلمة
الحكمة
الجهل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think Maturidi is discussing legitimate sacrifice, but he is using it to expose dualist contradictions.
Correction: Maturidi is not endorsing or condemning sacrifice itself; he is pointing out that the dualists' own logic would require them to permit it, contradicting their prohibition.

Checkpoint
According to Maturidi, why do the dualists' own principles make it impossible for them to seek wisdom?
A) Because they are too busy with debates.
B) Because evil cannot accept good, and ignorance is evil, so they cannot learn.
C) Because they have no books.
D) Because light is too bright.

Key Takeaway
The dualists' own principles lead to self-contradictions, making their worldview untenable.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you find yourself unable to understand why Allah allows certain hardships or evils in your life, do you ever entertain doubts that lead you away from tawheed? How does Maturidi's argument that 'inability to comprehend wisdom does not compel belief in two' challenge you to strengthen your reliance on Allah's oneness rather than questioning His wisdom?
?
Are there any seemingly 'good' actions in your life that stem from a source you consider 'evil'—like pride, selfishness, or hypocrisy? Maturidi exposes the dualists' inconsistency in separating good and evil into two essences. Examine your own heart: do you ever separate your actions from your intentions, claiming 'the action is good but the source is not'? How does that contradict the unity of the self that Islam calls you to purify?
?
The dualists' claim that 'the essence of light never brings evil' made them incapable of seeking knowledge because ignorance is evil. Reflect on a time you avoided learning something difficult or confronting an uncomfortable truth because you assumed it would lead to 'evil'—perhaps a weakness in your character or a sin you'd rather ignore. How does Maturidi's argument force you to see that avoiding knowledge is itself an evil, and that seeking truth—even when painful—is a form of worship?

Refutation of Dualist Two Principles
Maturidi refutes dualism with logical argument and defines wisdom.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's logical refutation of the dualist claim that good and evil originate from two separate eternal principles, and his explanation of divine wisdom.

Core Idea
Affirming two eternal principles leads to logical contradiction; apparent evil is part of a deeper wisdom.

Maturidi addresses the ancient dualist argument that good and evil must come from two separate sources. He demonstrates that this view is self-defeating and that what appears as evil may be an act of wisdom beyond human understanding. This lesson unpacks his reasoning step by step.

Guided Path
1
Identify the dualist claim
Understand the dualist claim: good and evil require two eternal principles. This is the view Maturidi refutes.
2
Follow the logical argument
Maturidi argues that each principle must be either knowing or ignorant. Trace how each option leads to contradiction or vanity.
Focus on the phrases 'مستمع له فهو عبث' and 'فبطل بحمد الله'.
3
Analyze the objection and response
The dualist objects: how can a wise being produce foolishness? Maturidi responds by distinguishing between essential and accidental wisdom, and by asserting that some wisdom is beyond human grasp.
Notice the definition of wisdom given at the end.
4
Understand the definition of wisdom
Maturidi defines wisdom as placing each thing in its proper place. This refutes the dualist's misunderstanding of God's actions.
5
Apply the lesson
Reflect on how this argument preserves divine unity and justice without denying the existence of evil in the world.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مستمع له فهو عبث . فلا بد من تحقيق الجهل والعلم في جوهر [كل] منها ليصح ذلك المعنى ، وفى ذلك جمع الأمرين في أحدهما ، وذلك المعنى ألزمهم القول باثنين ، فبطل بحمد الله
والأصل فيه أن التكلم منهم بالحكمة لا بالحكمة لا يعدو إما أن تكلموا إما أن تكلموا بجوهرهم وهو يعلم فيخرج مخرج العبث ، أو يجهله المكلَّم، وإيهما كان ففيه ثبات الأمرين من غير جوهره، فإنه لا يخلو أيضا من قبول أو عبث ، وأيهما كان من واحد أو ففى ذلك ما قلنا ، ولا قوة إلا بالله .
ثم يقال : إذ القول بأن لا يجوز أن يكون واحد يجي منه خير وشير ؛ ومن هذا قوله كيف كان منها العالم الذي كل واحد منهم هذا وصفه ، فينتقض عليها فعلها ما لذلك أدعى لما ذلك . أترى سفها أعظم مما عملاهما بأنفسها أو جهلا أبين من ذلك ، ولا قوة إلا بالله
فإن قال قائلهم : كيف زعمتم أنه يجوز أن يكون من الحكيم يجيء فعل السفه ؟ قلنا : هذا لا يجى ممن هو حكيم بذاته، إنما يجي ممن يجهل ، كما قلتم في النور من الجهل بعمل الظلمة ونحو ذلك . فأما الله سبحانه يتعالى عن ذلك . لكن قد يجوز أن يكون فعل حكمة لا يبلغها عقل البشر، وإلا فهو يجل عن ذلك . وما الحكمة إلا الإصابة فى أن يوضع كل شيء موضعه ، ويعطى كل ذي حظ حظه ، ولا يبخس بأحد حقه. وإنما أبى من يظن بالله أو بما يضيف إليه الموحدون ذلك ؛ لجهلهم بخدود الحكمة ومبلغ المحظوظ ، وايجابهم الحقوق لمن ليست لهم . وسنذكره إن شاء الله في موضع أملك به من هذا
If there is a listener, it is vain. So it is necessary to ascertain ignorance and knowledge in the substance of each of them for that meaning to be correct, and in that is combining the two matters in one of them, and that meaning compelled them to say two, so it is nullified, praise be to God. And the principle in it is that their speech about wisdom—about wisdom—is only that they either speak by their substance while it knows, so it comes out as vain, or the speaker is ignorant of it; whichever it is, in it is the confirmation of the two matters from other than its substance, for it is not free from either acceptance or vanity; whichever it is, from one or, and in that is what we said, and there is no power except with God. Then it is said: Since the statement that it is not permissible for one from whom come good and evil; and from this is His statement, how can the world be from them, each one of them being described this way; so their action is contradicted, for what is claimed? Do you see any foolishness greater than what both of them did to themselves, or any ignorance more apparent than that? And there is no power except with God. If their questioner says: How do you claim that it is permissible for the wise to produce an act of foolishness? We say: This does not come from one who is wise by essence; it only comes from one who is ignorant, as you said about light due to ignorance of the act of darkness and the like. As for God, exalted is He, He is above that. But it may be that an act of wisdom occurs that the intellect of humans does not grasp; otherwise He is exalted above that. And wisdom is nothing but correctness in placing each thing in its proper place, giving every deserving one his share, and not defrauding anyone of his right. They only rejected what the monotheists attribute to God due to their ignorance of the boundaries of wisdom and the extent of what is deserved, and their imposing rights on those who do not have them. And we will mention it, God willing, in a place more appropriate than this.
Source pages p. 636

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of the dualist claim that good and evil originate from two separate eternal principles. Maturidi begins by stating that if there is a listener (i.e., a recipient of the act), the act cannot be vain. Therefore, the two principles must be either knowing or ignorant in their substance. If they are knowing, their act would be vain; if ignorant, then the two matters (good and evil) are established from something other than their substance. This forces the dualist to affirm two principles. Maturidi then argues that it is impossible for one principle to produce both good and evil, and that the dualists' own example refutes them. He then addresses an objection: how can a wise being produce an act of foolishness? He explains that this only happens from one who is wise in some respect but ignorant in another, or due to ignorance. God is above that; what appears as foolishness may be an act of wisdom beyond human understanding. True wisdom is placing everything in its proper place and giving each its due. The rejection of this is due to ignorance of the boundaries of wisdom.

Key Terms
حكمة
سفه
عبث
جهل
علم
جوهر

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Evil must come from a separate eternal source because a good God cannot create evil.
Correction: Maturidi argues that claiming two eternal principles leads to logical contradiction. What appears as evil may be part of a wisdom beyond human understanding, and God's actions are just.

Checkpoint
According to Maturidi's argument, why does the dualist claim that two principles are necessary lead to contradiction?
A) Because both principles would be equally wise and good.
B) Because the two principles would each have to be either knowing or ignorant, leading to vanity or confirmation of both matters in one.
C) Because one principle cannot produce both good and evil without being foolish.
D) Because the dualists admit that their principles are ignorant.

Key Takeaway
The dualist claim of two eternal principles is self-contradictory; apparent evil is part of a wisdom beyond human grasp, and true wisdom is placing each thing in its proper place.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever, even for a moment, doubted God's wisdom because of an evil or tragedy you witnessed? The dualist says evil must come from a separate source; Maturidi calls that absurd. How does this lesson challenge you to see God's hand even in what you do not understand? What will you do the next time such doubt creeps in?
?
Maturidi defines wisdom as giving each thing its proper place. Think of one event in your life that felt unfair or foolish. Can you honestly say you believe God placed it correctly? If not, what would it cost you to submit to that truth? And if yes, how does that change your attitude toward that event today?
?
The objection in the passage says a wise being cannot produce foolishness. Have you ever accused God, even silently, of being unwise because of something that happened to you? This lesson says such an act is due to 'ignorance of the boundaries of wisdom.' Will you admit your own boundary? What specific step can you take to cultivate trust in God's wisdom beyond your understanding?

Introduction to al-Ka'bi's Views on Attributes
Maturidi introduces al-Ka'bi's views on attributes

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Maturidi discusses al-Ka'bi's views and what that reveals about the Mu'tazilite school.

Core Idea
Knowing al-Ka'bi's theology reveals the heart of Mu'tazilism.

Maturidi begins a new section by framing a question about al-Ka'bi's opinions on divine attributes. He explains that examining these views will show al-Ka'bi's level of knowledge about God and also provide insight into the Mu'tazilite school as a whole, since al-Ka'bi is their leading figure. This passage sets the stage for a critical refutation.

3

Maturidi says the purpose is to know al-Ka'bi's extent in knowledge of God and to comprehend the Mu'tazilite school. This shows that al-Ka'bi is considered their imam.

Metaphorical Attribution in Attributes: Objection and Response
Maturidi asserts permissibility of metaphor and answers an objection.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Maturidi position on metaphorical language in describing attributes and how they respond to the objection that this would allow attributes to have attributes.

Core Idea
Metaphor is permissible but reality is the basis.

Imam Maturidi addresses a common objection against using metaphorical language for divine attributes. He affirms that metaphor is allowed for certain expressions, but the real meaning remains unchanged. However, an objector argues that if metaphor is permitted, then an attribute could itself be described as having an attribute. Let's see how Maturidi responds.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation to understand the flow of the argument.
Notice the concise style of the objection and response.
2
Identify the statement about metaphor and the objection
Find where Maturidi states the permissibility of metaphor and where the objection is raised. Note the exact phrasing.
3
Understand the response and its implication
Maturidi's response distinguishes between description in speech and real attribution. Reflect on why this prevents an infinite regress.
This is a key point in defending the classical view of attributes.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم قال : وإنا لا ننكر جواز إطلاق القول بأن الحمرة صفة الأحمر ، والرحمة صفة الفعل ، لكن على المجاز ، والحقيقة ما ذكرت.
ثم عورض بأنه يجوز إذا أن يكون للصفة صفة.
قال : نعم، بمعنى أنها توصف، لكن ذلك إنما يوجد ما دام الواصف قائلا، فإذا أمسك لا.
قال الفقيه رحمه الله : تأملوا عظيم منزلة المعتزلة بهذا الذي هذا مبلغ علمه بالصفة والموصوف والمجاز والحقيقة مما لو قرن به أجهل أهل توحيد الله لاستعظمه ، ثم يقدم قومه يوم القيامة فيوردهم المورد الذى هذا وصف سبيله نسأل الله العصمة.
Then he said: We do not deny the permissibility of saying that redness is an attribute of the red, and mercy is an attribute of action, but metaphorically, while the reality is what I mentioned.
Then it was objected: If that is permissible, then it is possible for an attribute to have an attribute.
He said: Yes, in the sense that it is described, but that only exists as long as the describer is speaking; if he stops, then no.
Al-Faqih (may Allah have mercy on him) said: Reflect on the great position of the Mu'tazila with this, which is the extent of his knowledge of the attribute and the described, metaphor and reality; if the most ignorant of the people of Tawhid of Allah were compared to him, he would deem it immense. Then he will lead his people on the Day of Judgment and bring them to the place whose description is this — we ask Allah for protection.
Source pages p. 656

Commentary
This passage opens with a statement about metaphorical attribution. The speaker acknowledges that it is permissible to say 'redness is an attribute of the red' and 'mercy is an attribute of action' metaphorically, but the real meaning is as previously stated. An objection is then raised: if metaphor is allowed, then an attribute itself could have an attribute (e.g., redness could have a color attribute). The response is that something can be described only while the describer is speaking; when he stops, the description ceases. Then al-Faqih (the jurist) comments, criticizing the Mu'tazila. He says that if even an ignorant person of Tawhid were compared to this Mu'tazili scholar, he would be astonished at the Mu'tazili's ignorance regarding attributes, metaphor, and reality. He then warns that such a Mu'tazili will lead his followers to a terrible fate on the Day of Judgment, and we seek Allah's protection.

Key Terms
المجاز
الحقيقة
الصفة
الموصوف

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Metaphor in attributes means God's attributes are not real.
Correction: Metaphor is permissible in describing certain relationships, but the attributes themselves are real in the sense of haqiqa.

Checkpoint
What was the objection raised against the permissibility of metaphorical attribution?
A) That metaphor leads to anthropomorphism.
B) That an attribute could itself have an attribute.
C) That the Mu'tazila reject metaphor entirely.
D) That the attribute of action is not real.

Key Takeaway
Metaphorical language is permissible in describing attributes, but it does not imply that attributes have real attributes of their own.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you speak of God's mercy or love, do you treat them as mere metaphors or as real attributes that demand a response from you? How does that shape your trust and actions toward Him?
?
The passage distinguishes between metaphorical and real attribution. In your own life, where do you confuse metaphor for reality in your relationship with God—perhaps in how you describe His justice or His care? What would it take to live by the haqiqa?
?
The objection about attributes regressing infinitely is answered by stopping the description. Are there thoughts or questions about God that you keep 'describing' that actually lead you to doubt? How can you intentionally stop and rest in the reality He has revealed?

A Harsh Critique of the Mu'tazila's Ignorance
Al-Faqih criticizes the Mu'tazila's ignorance and warns of their punishment.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the severity of the Maturidi critique against the Mu'tazila regarding their inadequate understanding of attributes, metaphor, and reality.

Core Idea
Even the most ignorant monotheist would be shocked at Mu'tazili ignorance.

After refuting the objection, the commentator al-Faqih delivers a scathing rebuke of the Mu'tazila. He compares their knowledge of these fundamental kalam concepts to that of the most ignorant person of Tawhid, and warns of their dire fate on the Day of Judgment. This lesson unpacks the intensity of that critique.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic and translation to grasp the rhetorical tone. Notice the dramatic language.
This is a strong condemnation typical of classical polemical works.
2
Reflect on the comparison
Consider why al-Faqih uses the image of 'the most ignorant of the people of Tawhid' to highlight the Mu'tazili's deficiency.
This emphasizes how basic the Mu'tazili understanding is.
3
Consider the warning about the Day of Judgment
Think about the theological implications of leading followers to punishment due to erroneous beliefs.
Seeking Allah's protection (العصمة) is a common prayer in such contexts.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم قال : وإنا لا ننكر جواز إطلاق القول بأن الحمرة صفة الأحمر ، والرحمة صفة الفعل ، لكن على المجاز ، والحقيقة ما ذكرت.
ثم عورض بأنه يجوز إذا أن يكون للصفة صفة.
قال : نعم، بمعنى أنها توصف، لكن ذلك إنما يوجد ما دام الواصف قائلا، فإذا أمسك لا.
قال الفقيه رحمه الله : تأملوا عظيم منزلة المعتزلة بهذا الذي هذا مبلغ علمه بالصفة والموصوف والمجاز والحقيقة مما لو قرن به أجهل أهل توحيد الله لاستعظمه ، ثم يقدم قومه يوم القيامة فيوردهم المورد الذى هذا وصف سبيله نسأل الله العصمة.
Then he said: We do not deny the permissibility of saying that redness is an attribute of the red, and mercy is an attribute of action, but metaphorically, while the reality is what I mentioned.
Then it was objected: If that is permissible, then it is possible for an attribute to have an attribute.
He said: Yes, in the sense that it is described, but that only exists as long as the describer is speaking; if he stops, then no.
Al-Faqih (may Allah have mercy on him) said: Reflect on the great position of the Mu'tazila with this, which is the extent of his knowledge of the attribute and the described, metaphor and reality; if the most ignorant of the people of Tawhid of Allah were compared to him, he would deem it immense. Then he will lead his people on the Day of Judgment and bring them to the place whose description is this — we ask Allah for protection.
Source pages p. 656

Commentary
This passage opens with a statement about metaphorical attribution. The speaker acknowledges that it is permissible to say 'redness is an attribute of the red' and 'mercy is an attribute of action' metaphorically, but the real meaning is as previously stated. An objection is then raised: if metaphor is allowed, then an attribute itself could have an attribute (e.g., redness could have a color attribute). The response is that something can be described only while the describer is speaking; when he stops, the description ceases. Then al-Faqih (the jurist) comments, criticizing the Mu'tazila. He says that if even an ignorant person of Tawhid were compared to this Mu'tazili scholar, he would be astonished at the Mu'tazili's ignorance regarding attributes, metaphor, and reality. He then warns that such a Mu'tazili will lead his followers to a terrible fate on the Day of Judgment, and we seek Allah's protection.

Key Terms
المعتزلة
المجاز
الحقيقة
الصفة
الموصوف
توحيد

Key Terms from Source Passage

A) The Mu'tazili is much more knowledgeable.
B) They are equal.
C) The ignorant person would be astonished at the Mu'tazili's ignorance.
D) The Mu'tazili is like the ignorant person.

Key Takeaway
The Mu'tazila are severely criticized for their poor grasp of the concepts of attribute, metaphor, and reality, with a warning of punishment in the afterlife.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
How often do you assume your understanding of Allah's attributes is correct without comparing it to the most basic Tawhid? What steps will you take today to ensure you are not like those al-Faqih warned about?
?
The Mu'tazili will lead his people astray even unknowingly. Have you ever been complacent about learning correct 'aqeedah, thinking your current knowledge is enough? What will you change?
?
Al-Faqih asks us to reflect (تأملوا). Reflect on your own certainty about Allah's attributes. Is your belief based on revelation or your own reasoning? Commit to one action to verify your understanding.

Refutation of the Qaramita View
Maturidi refutes the Qaramita by extending an earlier argument.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Maturidi extends the refutation of the hylé view to the Qaramita sect's claim about the first originated being containing the world.

Core Idea
If the first being contains everything, it too needs a director — it is originated.

In this brief passage, Maturidi applies the same logic used against the hylé view to the Qaramita sect. The Qaramita claimed that the first originated being (al-mubda' al-awwal) contains the entire world in a manifest form. Maturidi points out that this claim suffers from the same problem: an originated being, however grand, still requires a cause and cannot be the ultimate source of all things. This lesson explains the parallel refutation.

Guided Path
1
Understand the Qaramita claim
The Qaramita believed the first created being (al-mubda' al-awwal) contains the entire world in a manifest form. Grasp what they asserted.
The term 'mubda' al-awwal' refers to the first originated entity in their cosmology.
2
See the parallel to the hylé argument
Recall the earlier refutation of the hylé (first matter) view. The same logic applies: any originated entity, even if it contains all things, is itself contingent and points to a necessary cause.
Maturidi uses 'kadhalika' (likewise) to explicitly link the arguments.
3
Grasp the conclusion
The Qaramita's claim does not escape the need for a Creator. Only the unoriginated, self-sufficient Being can be the source of all existence.
This reinforces the core Islamic principle of tawhid.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وكذلك يلزم القرامطة - في قولهم إن المبدع الأول فيه جميع العالم مبروزا
And likewise it applies to the Qaramita, in their claim that the first originated being contains all the world in a manifest form.
Source pages p. 662

Commentary
This brief passage extends the previous refutation to the Qaramita, a sect that believed the first created being (al-mubda' al-awwal) contains the entire world in a manifest way. Maturidi states that the same logic applies to them: if the first originated being contains everything, then it too requires a director, since it is originated and contingent. The argument is parallel to the refutation of the hylé view.

Key Takeaway
Maturidi's refutation of the Qaramita shows that no originated being, however grandiose, can be the source of all existence; only the unoriginated Creator can be.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

How does this lesson's reminder that every originated thing is contingent challenge you to shift your reliance back to the unoriginated Creator?
?
Have you ever held a belief about the universe that you thought was 'spiritual' but actually placed something originated in the place of the Creator? Will you take time today to examine your deepest assumptions and repent of anything that gives created things ultimate authority?
?
The Qaramita claimed their first being contained all truth. Do you ever treat your own mental constructs or ideologies as if they contain all truth? Will you humble yourself and submit to the only unoriginated Source of truth, regardless of how attractive your own ideas seem?
