Refuting the Batiniyya on God's Knowledge and Attributes
Maturidi refutes the Bātiniyya reinterpretation of divine names and its consequences.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Maturidi refutes the Batiniyya claim that divine names refer to Intellect and Soul, showing that it leads to ta'til (denial of attributes) and blind following.

Core Idea
Denying God's attributes to avoid tashbih actually empties worship of meaning.

The Batiniyya (esotericists) claimed that to preserve God's transcendence, we must reinterpret divine names as referring to created realities like Intellect and Soul. Maturidi shows that this move, far from protecting tawhid, actually destroys the very basis for knowing God. In this lesson, we'll follow his critique and see why the Batiniyya position collapses into blind following.

Guided Path
1
Understand the Batiniyya position
Read the passage to grasp how the Batiniyya reinterpret divine names as referring to Intellect and Soul, and how they claim everything is manifested in the Intellect.
Note that they use the concept of ibdāʿ (origination) to explain the relationship.
2
Identify Maturidi's critique
Maturidi points out a contradiction: if God originates without knowledge, will, and power, then origination becomes a natural, unconscious act like fire burning. Find where he makes this comparison.
3
Recognize the consequence: ta'til
Maturidi argues that this position leads to ta'til (denial of attributes), making God unknowable and their belief based on taqlid, not evidence.
This is the key doctrinal danger.
4
Reflect on the broader lesson
Think about why Maturidi insists on affirming attributes without tashbih. How does this preserve both transcendence and knowledge of God?

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقالت الباطنية وهم الذين يصرفون المذكور من الأسماء إلى المبدع الأول والثانى نمو العقل والنفس، ويجعلون كل العالم مبروزاً في العقل تستمد منه النفس فيمد الهيولى ، يقولون : كان العقل بالإبداع ، والإبداع علته ، مبروز فيه كل شيء يكون ، ومحال أن يُبرزه بالإبداع من لم يعلم ما يكون أو لم يعلم ما يكون أو من لا يقدر على أن يبرزه أو لا من يريد أن يكون مبروزا ، فيخرج الإبداع منه خروج فعل ذى طبع من حيث لا يشعر به ولا يعلمه ولا يوصف بالقدرة [عليه] ، فيكون الله تعالى عنده فى نفى الصفات عنه والأسماء كراهية التشبيه يصير في حد التعطيل ، ويصير بحيث لا شيء عليه بدليل ، ويحصل القول منه على التقليد ، وذلك بعيد ، والله الموفق .
And the Bāṭiniyya said—and they are those who divert the mentioned names to the First Originated and the Second, which are Intellect and Soul—and they make the entire world manifested in the Intellect, from which the Soul draws, then it extends to hyle (prime matter). They say: The Intellect came to be by origination (ibdāʿ), and origination is its cause; everything that will be is manifested in it. And it is impossible for one who does not know what will be, or who does not know what will be, or who is not able to manifest it, or who does not will that it be manifested, to manifest it by origination. So origination issues from Him like the act of one with a nature (dhū ṭabʿ), in that He is not aware of it, does not know it, and is not described with power over it. So God, according to him (the Bāṭinī), becomes—due to negating attributes and names from Him out of aversion to tashbih—in the realm of taʿṭīl (denial), and becomes such that nothing can be proven about Him by evidence, and his statement becomes based on taqlīd (blind following). And that is far (from truth). And Allah is the granter of success.
Source pages p. 693

Commentary
This passage is a refutation (radd) of the Bāṭiniyya (esotericists) who reinterpret divine names to refer to the First Originated (al-mubdaʿ al-awwal) and the Second (al-thānī), which they identify with Intellect ('aql) and Soul (nafs). They claim the entire world is manifested in the Intellect, and the Soul draws from it to extend to prime matter (hayūlā). Their doctrine holds that Intellect came into being by ibdāʿ (origination), and ibdāʿ is its cause. Everything that will exist is already present in the Intellect. Maturidi points out a contradiction: They say that God originated the Intellect without knowledge, power, or will, because they deny that God has attributes (ṣifāt) and names to avoid tashbih. But then the act of origination becomes like a natural, unconscious emanation—like fire burning without awareness. Maturidi argues that this leads to taʿṭīl (stripping God of attributes), making it impossible to prove anything about God. Consequently, their position is based on taqlīd, not rational evidence, which is far from truth. The passage ends with a prayer for divine success.

Key Terms
الباطنية (al-Bāṭiniyya)
إبداع (ibdāʿ)
عقل ('aql)
نفس (nafs)
هيولى (hayūlā)
تعطيل (taʿṭīl)
تقليد (taqlīd)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Denying divine names and attributes preserves tawhid.
Correction: Maturidi shows it leads to ta'til, making God unknowable and belief irrational.

Checkpoint
What does Maturidi accuse the Batiniyya of ultimately falling into by denying divine names and attributes?
A) Tashbih (anthropomorphism)
B) Ta'til (denial of attributes)
C) Shirk (polytheism)
D) Nifaq (hypocrisy)

Key Takeaway
The Batiniyya's attempt to preserve tawhid by denying attributes actually leads to ta'til, making God unknowable and their belief baseless.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever avoided affirming a divine attribute because it felt uncomfortable or seemed to resemble creation? How might this reflect a hidden fear that God is not truly transcendent unless stripped of all description? Will you commit today to affirming what God has affirmed for Himself, trusting that He is beyond any likeness?
?
Do you base your beliefs about God on solid evidence, or have you inherited some ideas without examination—perhaps even a subtle form of ta'til? What would it cost you to honestly test your convictions against the Quran and reason?
?
If God were unknowable (as the Batiniyya imply), how would your worship be affected? Would you still pray to a being you cannot know? Examine your heart: do you truly worship the God who has revealed His names, or an abstract concept you have constructed?

6 min
The Batiniyya's Self-Defeating Position on Divine Names
Exposing the polytheistic consequence of Batiniyya's denial.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the Batiniyya's attempt to avoid anthropomorphism by denying God a name leads to worshiping multiple entities, contradicting tawhid.

Core Idea
Denying God a name turns monotheism into worship of numbers.

The Batiniyya claimed that 'Allah' is the name of the Intellect and 'al-Rahman' the name of the Soul, refusing to ascribe a name to God to avoid anthropomorphism. Maturidi exposes the absurd consequences: this reduces the messengers' call to polytheism. This passage opens his dialectical refutation by showing the self-defeating nature of their view.

Guided Path
1
Read the Batiniyya's claim
Read carefully their assertion that 'Allah' refers to the Intellect and 'al-Rahman' to the Soul. Note that they deny God a name to avoid tashbih.
This is their starting point.
2
Identify the consequence
Notice how Maturidi says they end up with multiple objects of worship (Intellect, Soul, etc.), turning the messengers' call into worship of number, not tawhid.
This is the reductio ad absurdum.
3
Reflect on the core problem
Ask yourself: why does denying God a name lead to polytheism? Because they still use names like 'al-Ilah' for the worshipped one.
The contradiction is exposed.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مع ما يقال : الله اسمه أو هو اسم غيره ، فيرجع في الحقيقة إلى أنه اسم العقل ، والرحمن اسم النفس ، وعلى هذا مذهبهم. وأبوا الاسم كراهة التشبيه ، ثم جعلوا المعبود باسم الإله ، والرحمن والرحيم أغيارا لا يحصى عددهم ، وأجزاء يصعب إحصاؤهم ، فكأن الرسل جاءوا عندهم بعبادة العدد لا بالتوحيد ، والله المستعان . ثم يقال لهم عند قولهم ليس له اسم ، ما تعنون بقولكم ليس له ، اسم ذاتى ولا صفة ذاتية ؟ فلا يجدون السبيل إلى أن يعبروا عن أنفسهم بما قالوا ليس له اسم، ويبطل عملتهم الذي قالوا : الله الذى ليس له اسم ذاتى ، ثم زعموا أن له اسما من غيره نحو المبدع بإبداع هو علة المبدع هو العال لا المعلول ولا علة ؛ لأن كل معلول يجوز أن يصير فيقال / له إذ جعل اسمه عن غيره : أكان ما حقق له غيره ذلك الا. أو سُمّى [به] ؟ فإن قال : لا ، له أن يسميه ما شاء من الأغيار، والعلـــة ۲۰ والمعلول ؛ إذ بغيره استحق ، لأنه كذلك يقول : كان ولا علة ولا معلول ، فإذا هو قول كان بحق المجاز لا بالحقيقة بالضرورة ، فأوجب هذا الاسم له غيره من غير أن كان منه ما استوجب. فإن قال : كان منه الإبداع ، قيل : كان منه الإبداع بعد أن لم يكن ، حتى حقق له الاسم بأن كان به من أى وجه ، حتى سم
Along with what is said: 'Allah is His name, or it is the name of another', so it returns in reality to the name of the Intellect, and al-Rahman is the name of the Soul, and this is their doctrine. And they refused the name out of dislike for tashbih, then they made the worshipped one under the name 'al-Ilah', and al-Rahman and al-Rahim as others whose number cannot be counted, and parts that are difficult to enumerate. So it is as if the messengers came, according to them, with the worship of number, not with tawhid. And Allah is the sought help. Then it is said to them, when they say 'He has no name': What do you mean by your saying 'He has no name'? An essential name or an essential attribute? Then they cannot find a way to express themselves regarding what they said 'He has no name', and their claim that 'Allah is the one who has no essential name' is invalidated. Then they claimed that He has a name from something other than Him, such as 'al-Mubdiʿ' (the Originator) by an origination that is the cause of the originated, He is the 'āl (?) not the effect and not the cause; because every effect may become—it is said to him when he made His name from another: Did what another gave Him come to be? Or was He named [by it]? If he says: 'No', he can name Him whatever he wishes from others, and the cause and the effect; since by another He deserved (the name), because he also says: 'He was without cause and without effect'. So it is a statement that was by way of metaphor, not by reality necessarily. So this name necessitates that another gives it to Him without His having done anything to deserve it. If he says: 'Origination came from Him', it is said: 'Origination came from Him after it was not, until the name was realized for Him that He was by it from some aspect, until He was named'.
Source pages p. 694

Commentary
This passage is a dialectical refutation (jadal) of the Bāṭiniyya position on divine names. Maturidi first notes that they claim 'Allah' is the name of the Intellect and 'al-Rahman' is the name of the Soul, thereby reducing the divine names to created entities. Despite rejecting names to avoid tashbih, they end up with multiple objects of worship (Intellect, Soul, etc.), making the messengers call to polytheism (worship of number) rather than tawhid. Then Maturidi poses a logical dilemma: If they say God has no name, do they mean no essential name (ism dhātī) or no essential attribute (ṣifa dhātiyya)? They cannot answer coherently, invalidating their position. They then claim God has a name derived from something else, like 'al-Mubdiʿ' (Originator) because of origination (ibdāʿ). Maturidi questions: If the name comes from another, did that other truly give it? If they say God existed without cause and effect, then the name is merely metaphorical (majāz), not real. Moreover, if they say origination came from God, it must have occurred after not existing, which implies change—so the name is only acquired, not essential. The argument is left slightly incomplete at the end of the window.

Key Terms
اسم ذاتى (ism dhātī)
صفة ذاتية (ṣifa dhātiyya)
مجاز (majāz)
إبداع (ibdāʿ)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Denying God an essential name preserves His transcendence.
Correction: Maturidi shows this leads to worshiping multiple entities, thus undermining tawhid.

Checkpoint
According to Maturidi, what is the consequence of the Batiniyya's view on divine names?
A) It protects tawhid from anthropomorphism.
B) It leads to worshiping a single being called 'Intellect'.
C) It results in worshiping many entities, making the messengers call to polytheism.
D) It is the correct position of the early Muslims.

Key Takeaway
Denying God a name while still using names for created entities turns monotheism into polytheism.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you read that the Batiniyya denied God a name to avoid tashbih, ask yourself: Have I ever been more concerned with protecting God from my own mental images than with accepting His names as He revealed them? What does my heart’s resistance to a particular divine name reveal about my trust in His revelation?
?
The Batiniyya ended up worshipping 'numbers'—created concepts they treated as divine. In your life, what 'numbers' (such as success, comfort, reputation, or relationships) have you subtly elevated to a place where they rival God in your devotion? Identify one today and decide how to dethrone it.
?
Imagine you are standing before Allah and He asks: 'Did you worship Me alone, or did you serve a god of your own invention?' After studying this passage, what specific step will you take this week to ensure you are worshiping the God who named Himself, rather than a god shaped by your own fears or preferences?

8 min
The Logical Dilemma on Essential vs. Acquired Names
Logical trap and analysis of metaphorical naming.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's logical trap for the Batiniyya: if God has no name, do you mean no essential name or no essential attribute? And if He has a name from another, it is merely metaphorical.

Core Idea
Either God has an essential name or all names are metaphors from creation.

Maturidi presses the Batiniyya further: when you say 'He has no name', what exactly are you denying? An essential name or an essential attribute? They cannot answer consistently. Then he examines their claim that God has a name derived from something else (like al-Mubdi')—showing it leads to metaphorical naming, not real divine naming.

Guided Path
1
Understand the question
Maturidi asks: what do you mean by 'He has no name'? Essential name or essential attribute? This forces them to clarify.
They cannot respond coherently.
2
See the invalidation
Their inability to answer invalidates their whole position on divine names.
This is a dialectical knockout.
3
Analyze the naming from another
If they say He has a name from something else (like 'Originator' from origination), then the name is dependent on creation, making it metaphorical.
This reduces the name to a metaphor.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مع ما يقال : الله اسمه أو هو اسم غيره ، فيرجع في الحقيقة إلى أنه اسم العقل ، والرحمن اسم النفس ، وعلى هذا مذهبهم. وأبوا الاسم كراهة التشبيه ، ثم جعلوا المعبود باسم الإله ، والرحمن والرحيم أغيارا لا يحصى عددهم ، وأجزاء يصعب إحصاؤهم ، فكأن الرسل جاءوا عندهم بعبادة العدد لا بالتوحيد ، والله المستعان . ثم يقال لهم عند قولهم ليس له اسم ، ما تعنون بقولكم ليس له ، اسم ذاتى ولا صفة ذاتية ؟ فلا يجدون السبيل إلى أن يعبروا عن أنفسهم بما قالوا ليس له اسم، ويبطل عملتهم الذي قالوا : الله الذى ليس له اسم ذاتى ، ثم زعموا أن له اسما من غيره نحو المبدع بإبداع هو علة المبدع هو العال لا المعلول ولا علة ؛ لأن كل معلول يجوز أن يصير فيقال / له إذ جعل اسمه عن غيره : أكان ما حقق له غيره ذلك الا. أو سُمّى [به] ؟ فإن قال : لا ، له أن يسميه ما شاء من الأغيار، والعلـــة ۲۰ والمعلول ؛ إذ بغيره استحق ، لأنه كذلك يقول : كان ولا علة ولا معلول ، فإذا هو قول كان بحق المجاز لا بالحقيقة بالضرورة ، فأوجب هذا الاسم له غيره من غير أن كان منه ما استوجب. فإن قال : كان منه الإبداع ، قيل : كان منه الإبداع بعد أن لم يكن ، حتى حقق له الاسم بأن كان به من أى وجه ، حتى سم

Key Terms

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: God can have a name derived from creation without implying change.
Correction: Maturidi shows that if the name arises from an act (like origination), that act occurred in time, so the name is not essential but acquired.

Checkpoint
What is the result when Maturidi asks whether the Batiniyya mean 'essential name' or 'essential attribute'?
A) They correctly answer 'neither'.
B) They cannot find a way to express themselves and their claim is invalidated.
C) They claim both are essential.
D) They deny the question.

Key Takeaway
Any claim that God has no essential name leads to inconsistency; any claimed name from creation is merely metaphorical.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you call upon Allah by names like 'Ar-Rahman' or 'Al-Khaliq', do you perceive them as mere descriptions of His actions, or as windows into Who He truly is in His essence? How does Maturidi's distinction between essential and metaphorical names move you to examine the depth of your own invocation?
?
If someone were to claim that 'Allah' is just a title we invent for convenience, would you have the clarity and conviction to refute them using the logic in this passage? What does your current level of understanding reveal about your own certainty in what the Name 'Allah' truly means?
?
Are there names or attributes of God that you recite without contemplating whether they are essential or derived? How does this lesson expose the spiritual danger of using God's names carelessly, and what concrete step can you take today to approach His names with greater reverence and precision?

The Mu'tazilite 'Most Beneficial' Argument Refuted
Maturidi refutes the Mu'tazilite 'most beneficial' argument.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the Mu'tazilite concept of 'most beneficial' is flawed and how Maturidi deconstructs it.

Core Idea
What is 'most beneficial' for creation could equally be most corrupt.

The Mu'tazila argued that God creates because He sees what is most beneficial. Maturidi challenges this by showing that the very condition supposed to ensure benefit could produce the greatest corruption. True wisdom is not about benefit to others but about putting everything in its proper place.

Guided Path
1
Read the Mu'tazilite claim
Read the opening statement by the Mu'tazila: God saw the most beneficial and did it, and He is not asked about that.
Note the two possible interpretations of 'most beneficial' that Maturidi considers.
2
Understand Maturidi's critique
Notice how Maturidi argues that if 'most beneficial' means wisdom, it's acceptable; if it means something else, it leads to absurdity because the condition for benefit could be the condition for corruption.
3
Contrast with true wisdom
Maturidi defines wisdom as correctness—putting everything in its proper place, which is justice. God's actions never depart from that.
This establishes an objective standard beyond mere benefit.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقال قوم من المعتزلة : رأى الأصلح كذلك ففعل ، ولا يسأل عن فعله الأصلح. قال الشيخ رحمه الله : وهذا كلام لا يخلو من أن يراد بالأصلح الحكمة ، فهو الأول . وإن أراد به معنى سواه فإن القول في معرفة الأصلح كهو في أنه لم فعل ، سواء ، مع ما يُسأل عن شرط الأصلح له في الفعل من أين يجب ؟ على أن أحق الناس بالاستحياء من هذا اللفظ هم ؛ إذ ليس من شيء يجعل [١٤٧] شرطا للأصلح إلا وأمكن أن يكون ذلك بعينه شرطا للفساد ، ويكون به أعظم الفساد ، ولا يجوز أن يكون شيء حكمة يصير سفها ؛ لأن تأويل الأصلح أن يكون أصلح لغيره ، وقد يكون به الفساد عندهم ، وتأويل الحكمة الإصابة ، وهو وضع كل شيء موضعه ، وذلك معنى العدل ، ولا يخرج فعله عن ذلك ، وقال : إن الله خالق بذاته ؛ إذ هو اسم المدح والعظمة ، ومخال أن يكون الله سبحانه يستحقه بغيره ؛ لما فيه ايجاب النفع له ، ومن ذلك وصف فعله فهو محتاج وإذ قد ثبت أنه خالق بذاته لم يجز أن لا يكون خالقا البته ، والسؤال عن لم محال كالسؤال عن لم قدر ؟ ولم علم ؟ ولا قوة إلا بالله .
And a group of the Mu'tazila said: He saw the most beneficial (al-aslah) and so did it, and He is not asked about His action that is most beneficial. The Shaykh, may Allah have mercy on him, said: This statement is not free from the possibility that by 'most beneficial' is meant wisdom, and that is the first. But if he intends a meaning other than that, then the saying concerning knowledge of the most beneficial is the same as the saying concerning why He did it, equally, along with the fact that He is asked concerning the condition of the most beneficial for Him in the action: from where is it obligatory? Indeed, the people most deserving of shame from this expression are they; for there is nothing that is made a condition for the most beneficial except that it is possible that that very thing be a condition for corruption, and thereby be the greatest corruption. It is not possible that something be wisdom and become foolishness, because the interpretation of 'most beneficial' is that it is beneficial for another, and it may cause corruption according to them, and the interpretation of wisdom is correctness, which is putting everything in its proper place, and that is the meaning of justice, and His action does not depart from that. And He said: Indeed, Allah is the Creator by His essence, because that is a name of praise and greatness, and it is inconceivable that Allah, exalted be He, deserve it by other than Himself, because that would entail benefit for Him. And from that, describing His action would imply that He is in need. And since it is established that He is Creator by His essence, it is not permissible that He not be a creator at all. And the question concerning 'why' is as impossible as the question concerning 'why He determined?' and 'why He knew?' And there is no power except with Allah.
Source pages p. 696

Commentary
This passage presents the Mu'tazilite view that God created because He saw the most beneficial (الأصلح) and then did it, and that He is not asked about that. Maturidi refutes this. He first notes that if 'most beneficial' simply means wisdom, then it reduces to the earlier position. If it means something else, then the problem remains: how do we know what is most beneficial? It becomes similar to the original question. Moreover, whatever is held as a condition for something to be most beneficial could equally be a condition for corruption, thus being the greatest evil. True wisdom is correctness (الإصابة), putting everything in its proper place, which is justice (العدل). God's actions never depart from that. Maturidi then reiterates the argument that God is Creator by essence (بذاته), a name of praise, so He never ceases to be Creator. The question 'why' for God's creating is as meaningless as asking 'why He willed?' or 'why He knew?', because these are essential attributes.

Key Terms
الأصلح
الحكمة
الإصابة
العدل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that 'most beneficial' provides a valid reason for God's creation.
Correction: Maturidi shows that the very condition for benefit could be the condition for corruption, making the concept unreliable.

Checkpoint
Why does Maturidi reject the Mu'tazilite 'most beneficial' argument?
A) Because it implies God is not wise.
B) Because whatever is made a condition for most beneficial could also be a condition for corruption.
C) Because the Mu'tazila are always wrong.
D) Because God does not do beneficial things.

Key Takeaway
Maturidi exposes the instability of the 'most beneficial' concept by showing that any condition for benefit could be a condition for corruption.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you face a difficult situation, do you ever catch yourself questioning whether this is truly 'most beneficial' for you? How does Maturidi's argument challenge the assumption that you even know what is beneficial?
?
If whatever you consider 'good' could just as easily be 'corrupt' from another angle, how does that affect your trust in your own judgments—and your reliance on Allah's wisdom instead?
?
This lesson equates wisdom with putting everything in its proper place. Is there an area of your life where you are trying to force something into a place it doesn't belong, insisting it would be 'best'? What would it look like to surrender that to Allah's justice today?

Allah is Creator by Essence; the Question 'Why' is Impossible
Maturidi argues Allah is Creator by essence and the question 'why' is invalid.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Maturidi affirms that Allah is Creator by His essence and why asking 'why' about His actions is meaningless.

Core Idea
Allah's creativity is essential, not for any external reason.

Maturidi concludes his refutation by affirming that Allah is the Creator by His own essence. This is a name of praise, and it would be contradictory for Him to ever cease creating. Furthermore, asking 'why' He creates is as absurd as asking 'why' He wills or knows.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Focus on the claim that Allah is Creator by essence.
Notice the reasoning: it is a name of praise and cannot be earned from others.
2
Understand 'Creator by essence'
Maturidi argues that Allah is Creator by His own essence, not by any external cause. This means creating is an essential attribute, not a contingent act.
3
Grasp the impossibility of 'why'
Asking 'why' Allah creates is as meaningless as asking 'why' He willed or knew, because these are essential attributes. The question itself is invalid.
This parallels the earlier argument about the impossibility of asking about divine attributes.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقال قوم من المعتزلة : رأى الأصلح كذلك ففعل ، ولا يسأل عن فعله الأصلح. قال الشيخ رحمه الله : وهذا كلام لا يخلو من أن يراد بالأصلح الحكمة ، فهو الأول . وإن أراد به معنى سواه فإن القول في معرفة الأصلح كهو في أنه لم فعل ، سواء ، مع ما يُسأل عن شرط الأصلح له في الفعل من أين يجب ؟ على أن أحق الناس بالاستحياء من هذا اللفظ هم ؛ إذ ليس من شيء يجعل [١٤٧] شرطا للأصلح إلا وأمكن أن يكون ذلك بعينه شرطا للفساد ، ويكون به أعظم الفساد ، ولا يجوز أن يكون شيء حكمة يصير سفها ؛ لأن تأويل الأصلح أن يكون أصلح لغيره ، وقد يكون به الفساد عندهم ، وتأويل الحكمة الإصابة ، وهو وضع كل شيء موضعه ، وذلك معنى العدل ، ولا يخرج فعله عن ذلك ، وقال : إن الله خالق بذاته ؛ إذ هو اسم المدح والعظمة ، ومخال أن يكون الله سبحانه يستحقه بغيره ؛ لما فيه ايجاب النفع له ، ومن ذلك وصف فعله فهو محتاج وإذ قد ثبت أنه خالق بذاته لم يجز أن لا يكون خالقا البته ، والسؤال عن لم محال كالسؤال عن لم قدر ؟ ولم علم ؟ ولا قوة إلا بالله .

It is not possible that something be wisdom and become foolishness, because the interpretation of 'most beneficial' is that it is beneficial for another, and it may cause corruption according to them, and the interpretation of wisdom is correctness, which is putting everything in its proper place, and that is the meaning of justice, and His action does not depart from that. And from that, describing His action would imply that He is in need. And since it is established that He is Creator by His essence, it is not permissible that He not be a creator at all. And the question concerning 'why' is as impossible as the question concerning 'why He determined?' and 'why He knew?' And there is no power except with Allah.
Source pages p. 696

Commentary
This passage presents the Mu'tazilite view that God created because He saw the most beneficial (الأصلح) and then did it, and that He is not asked about that. Maturidi refutes this. He first notes that if 'most beneficial' simply means wisdom, then it reduces to the earlier position. If it means something else, then the problem remains: how do we know what is most beneficial? Correction: Maturidi argues that creation is essential to Allah, not for any external reason, and asking 'why' is invalid.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

What would it mean to truly internalize that Allah is Creator by essence, not for any benefit to Himself?
?
When you face hardships, do you find yourself asking 'Why, Allah?' as if His actions need a reason beyond His essence? How would your patience and reliance increase if you accepted that asking 'why' is as impossible as questioning why He knows everything?
?
If Allah's creation is essential and not contingent on anything, then your very existence is a direct manifestation of His essence—not a favor to you or a response to your worth. Does this humble you or frighten you? How does this reality change the way you approach Him in prayer?

Maturidi's Critique of al-Aṣlaḥ
Maturidi refutes the Mu'tazila concept of al-aslah

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's critique of the Mu'tazila concept of al-aṣlaḥ (the most beneficial) and why he argues it either amounts to wisdom or is self-defeating.

Core Idea
The Mu'tazila's 'most beneficial' either is wisdom or leads to corruption.

This lesson examines a key debate in Islamic theology: whether God is obligated to do what is most beneficial for creation. The Mu'tazila affirm this, but Imam Maturidi offers a sharp critique, arguing that the concept is either redundant with divine wisdom or logically flawed. We will explore his reasoning step by step.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing it, then read the English translation. Let the argument unfold naturally.
Pay attention to the terms 'al-aṣlaḥ' (الأصلح) and 'al-ḥikmah' (الحكمة).
2
Identify the Mu'tazila claim
The Mu'tazila assert that God does what is most beneficial and is not questioned about it. Note the phrase 'ولا يسأل عن فعله الأصلح'.
3
Follow Maturidi's two-part analysis
Maturidi first considers if 'al-aṣlaḥ' means wisdom—if so, it's acceptable but repetitive. Then he considers an alternative meaning, leading to a deeper critique.
4
Examine the deeper critique
Maturidi argues that any condition for al-aṣlaḥ could also be a condition for corruption, making it unstable. Wisdom, by contrast, is defined as placing things in their proper places and never becomes foolishness.
This ties wisdom to justice (al-ʿadl).
5
Reflect on the conclusion
Maturidi concludes that God's action never departs from wisdom and justice, so the Mu'tazila concept is either unnecessary or flawed.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقال قوم من المعتزلة : رأى الأصلح كذلك ففعل ، ولا يسأل عن فعله الأصلح. قال الشيخ رحمه الله : وهذا كلام لا يخلو من أن يراد بالأصلح الحكمة ، فهو الأول . وإن أراد به معنى سواه فإن القول في معرفة الأصلح كهو في أنه لم فعل ، سواء ، مع ما يُسأل عن شرط الأصلح له في الفعل من أين يجب ؟ على أن أحق الناس بالاستحياء من هذا اللفظ هم ؛ إذ ليس من شيء يجعل شرطا للأصلح إلا وأمكن أن يكون ذلك بعينه شرطا للفساد ، ويكون به أعظم الفساد ، ولا يجوز أن يكون شيء حكمة يصير سفها ؛ لأن تأويل الأصلح أن يكون أصلح لغيره ، وقد يكون به الفساد عندهم ، وتأويل الحكمة الإصابة ، وهو وضع كل شيء موضعه ، وذلك معنى العدل ، ولا يخرج فعله عن ذلك .
And a group of the Mu'tazila said: He saw the most beneficial (al-aṣlaḥ) likewise and so acted, and He is not questioned about His action that is most beneficial. The Shaykh (Maturidi), may Allah have mercy on him, said: This statement is not free from [the possibility that] what is intended by 'the most beneficial' is wisdom, then it is the first [meaning]. But if he intends by it another meaning, then the discussion about knowing the most beneficial is the same as the discussion about why He acted, equal, along with the question: from where does the condition of the most beneficial for Him in the action become obligatory? Moreover, the people most entitled to be ashamed of this expression are they themselves; for there is nothing that is made a condition for the most beneficial except that it is possible that the very same thing is a condition for corruption, and by it there would be the greatest corruption. And it is not permissible that something [that is] wisdom should become foolishness; because the interpretation of 'the most beneficial' is that it is beneficial for other than Him, and it may involve corruption according to them, while the interpretation of 'wisdom' (al-ḥikmah) is correctness, and it is placing everything in its proper place, and that is the meaning of justice (al-ʿadl), and His action does not depart from that.
Source pages p. 696

Commentary
This passage contains an objection from the Mu'tazila and Maturidi's answer. The Mu'tazila claim that God does what is most beneficial (al-aṣlaḥ) and is not asked about it. Maturidi analyzes: If 'al-aṣlaḥ' means wisdom (ḥikmah), it's the same as the previous argument. If it means something else, then the debate about what is most beneficial is identical to the original question 'why did He act?'—it just pushes the problem back. Moreover, anything that could be a condition for al-aṣlaḥ could also be a condition for corruption (fasād), so it is not a stable criterion. Wisdom, by contrast, is defined as placing things in their proper places (waḍʿu kulli shayʾin mawḍiʿahu), which is justice (ʿadl) and cannot turn into foolishness. Thus the Mu'tazila's concept is problematic.

Key Terms
الأصلح
الحكمة
الفساد
العدل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: God is obligated to always do what is most beneficial for humans.
Correction: Maturidi argues that 'most beneficial' is not a stable criterion; it could also justify corruption. God's actions are based on wisdom and justice, which are inherently correct and do not rely on human notions of benefit.

Checkpoint
What is Maturidi's main critique of the Mu'tazila's concept of al-aṣlaḥ?
A) It contradicts the Quran.
B) It is either redundant with wisdom or leads to an infinite regress and possible corruption.
C) It implies God is ignorant.
D) It denies God's power.

Key Takeaway
Maturidi argues that the Mu'tazila concept of al-aṣlaḥ is either redundant with divine wisdom or leads to logical contradictions, and that true wisdom is synonymous with justice and cannot become foolishness.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you find yourself insisting that God must give you what seems 'most beneficial' according to your own judgment, which part of Maturidi's argument exposes the weakness of that demand? What does it reveal about your trust in His wisdom?
?
Maturidi says that any condition for benefit could equally be a condition for corruption. Think of a situation where you thought something was good for you but it actually caused harm. How does this realization challenge you to surrender your own assessments of benefit to God's placement of everything in its proper place?
?
Wisdom is defined as placing everything where it belongs—that is justice. If you truly believe that, how would it change your reaction to the trials and losses in your life? What specific area of your life do you most resist accepting as 'placed rightly' by God?

Classification of Interpretations of 'Who Knows Himself Knows His Lord'
Maturidi enumerates four sectarian misinterpretations of the saying.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how different sects misinterpret the famous saying 'Who knows himself knows his Lord' and why their interpretations are flawed.

Core Idea
The saying is unanimously accepted, but its meaning is twisted by each sect to fit their own theology.

Imam Maturidi presents a famous prophetic saying that is agreed upon by all Muslims, yet he notes that different groups have used it to support their own erroneous doctrines. In this passage, he summarizes four misinterpretations: the Dualists, the Jews, the Anthropomorphists, and Jahm. Understanding these misreadings prepares us for the correct interpretation that Maturidi will later explain.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing, then read the translation. Familiarize yourself with the four groups and their claims.
Notice that the passage begins with a statement of consensus.
2
Identify each sect's logic
For each group, identify how they define 'knowing oneself' and how that leads to a specific conception of God. Compare their reasoning.
The Dualists focus on good and evil, the Jews on unity, the Anthropomorphists on physicality, and Jahm on origination.
3
Reflect on the common error
Consider why all these interpretations are insufficient. What do they project onto God that should not be projected?
They all take a partial human experience and apply it directly to God.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قال قائل: أجمع أن من عرف نفسه عرف ربه، لكنهم اختلفوا في وجه المعرفة: فقالت الثنوية: لما عرف اشتمال نفسه على الخير والشر عرف أن لكل جهة منه رباً. واليهود صيرته واحداً جزءاً. وقالت المشبهة: هو جسم؛ إذ في الشاهد تكون معرفة النفس للجسم. وقال جهم: إذ عرف أنه كان بعد أن لم يكن، وهو شيء، جسم، عالم سميع بصير، علم أن كل ما كان له ذلك الاسم فهو حدث، وربه الذي أنشأه لا يحتمل أن يكون حدثا.
For he said: There is consensus that whoever knows himself knows his Lord, but they differed regarding the aspect of knowledge. The Dualists said: When he knew that his self comprises good and evil, he knew that for every aspect of him there is a lord. The Jews made Him one part. The Anthropomorphists said: He is a body, since in the witnessed world knowledge of the self is of the body. Jahm said: When he knew that he came to be after not being, and he is a thing, a body, knowing, hearing, seeing, he knew that everything that bears that name is originated, and his Lord who created him cannot be originated.
Source pages p. 701

Commentary
This passage is a classification. Maturidi presents the statement that 'Whoever knows himself knows his Lord' is agreed upon, but different sects interpret 'knowledge of oneself' differently. The Dualists (الثنوية) argue that knowledge of the self's composition of good and evil leads to knowing two lords corresponding to the two aspects. The Jews make God a single part (perhaps meaning a part of the world or a single component). The Anthropomorphists (المشبهة) assert that God is a body because self-knowledge in the physical world is knowledge of a body. Jahm (جهم) argues that self-knowledge reveals one's origination, so God, as the originator, cannot be originated. These varied misinterpretations demonstrate that the correct understanding requires deeper reflection, which Maturidi will provide.

Key Terms
من عرف نفسه عرف ربه
الثنوية
المشبهة
جهم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The saying 'Who knows himself knows his Lord' supports dualism, anthropomorphism, or other erroneous views about God.
Correction: The saying is agreed upon, but its true meaning is not any of these partial interpretations; each sect misuses it to justify its own theology.

Checkpoint
According to this passage, what did the Dualists (الثنوية) argue about self-knowledge?
A) It leads to knowing God is one.
B) It leads to knowing two lords.
C) It leads to knowing God is a body.
D) It leads to knowing God is not originated.

Key Takeaway
Different sects misuse the saying 'Who knows himself knows his Lord' to justify their own theological errors, but each misreading distorts the true meaning.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Which of these four misinterpretations resonates most with a view you have held or heard about God? How does seeing it listed as an error challenge you to examine your own understanding of who God is?
?
If you were to honestly ask yourself, 'In what ways have I unknowingly made God in my own image?', what would your answer be? Are you willing to let that image go, even if it feels uncomfortable?
?
The saying 'Who knows himself knows his Lord' is often used to claim a direct, personal knowledge of God. But this passage shows that self-knowledge can lead to grave errors. Where in your life have you assumed that your own experience or reasoning is enough to understand God, without submitting to revelation? Will you repent of that today?

Refutation of Jahm: God Does Not Acquire Attributes
Maturidi invalidates the view that God acquires attributes after not having them.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Maturidi invalidates the claim that God acquires attributes after not having them, based on the principle that change implies createdness.

Core Idea
Change in God is impossible; it would make Him like His creation.

Maturidi uses the preceding argument to reject the view of Jahm and others who claimed that God was not knowing, powerful, agent, or speaker and then became so. Such a view implies change in God, which is a characteristic of contingent beings. This lesson explores the first part of the refutation.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage
Read the Arabic text slowly, noting the key phrases that describe the invalidated views.
2
Identify the claims invalidated
Maturidi invalidates the claim that God was not knowing/powerful then became so, and that He was not agent/speaker then became so.
These are the views of Jahm and others.
3
Understand the reasoning
The reasoning is that allowing change in God would make Him subject to the same changes that indicate createdness in humans.
Change (taghayyur) is a sign of being created.
4
Reflect on the implication
If God changed, He would be like His creation, which contradicts His perfection and necessity.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وعلى هذا يبطل قول جهم: إنه لم يكن عالما قادرا ثم صار كذلك، وقول من يقول: لم يكن فاعلا متكلما ثم صار كذلك؛ إذ مكنوا فيه تغير الجهات والأحوال التى هى سبب معرفة العبد نفسه خلقا وحدثا، ولا قوة إلا بالله. و بما ذكرت من إمكان قبول الأحوال اختيارا، واحتماله الصفات العليه من نحو العلم والقدرة والحياة والسمع والبصر ما يوضح كونه بالحمانع العليم، لا بالطبائع التي هي عاجزة عن الاختيار وجاهلة بالأحوال، وكذلك جميع الأغذية، ولا قوة إلا بالله. وكذلك باحتماله الخير والشر ومختلف الأحوال دليل صرف تدبيره إلى من لا يوصف بالاحتمال ولا بمختلف الأحوال، ليكون كل شيء على ما عليه تقديره ولا قوة إلا بالله. وقال قوم: من عرف نفسه الخفية عرف ربه، ونفسه الخفية هي الكيان.
And on this basis the statement of Jahm is invalidated: that He was not knowing and powerful then became so, and the statement of those who say: He was not an agent or speaker then became so; for they allowed in Him change of aspects and states which are the cause of the servant's knowledge of himself as created and originated. And there is no power except with God. And by what I mentioned of the possibility of states through choice, and his bearing of the high attributes of knowledge, power, life, hearing, and sight, it clarifies that He is the [؟] the all-knowing, not by natures which are incapable of choice and ignorant of states, and likewise all foods. And there is no power except with God. And likewise, by his bearing good and evil and various states is proof that his management is turned over to one who is not described by bearing (potentiality) nor by varying states, so that everything may be as his decree requires. And there is no power except with God. And some people said: whoever knows his hidden self knows his Lord, and his hidden self is the entity (kayān).
Source pages p. 701

Commentary
This passage contains a refutation and an additional view. Maturidi uses the previous argument to invalidate the claims of Jahm and others that God acquires attributes like knowledge and power after not having them, or becomes an agent or speaker after not being so. Such claims imply change in God, which contradicts the self-knowledge that shows change is due to createdness and need. Maturidi then argues that the potential to accept attributes through choice—such as knowledge, power, life, hearing, sight—shows that God is all-knowing and not like blind natures that cannot choose. He also argues that the ability to bear good and evil and varying states indicates that the true manager is beyond change and potentiality. Finally, he mentions a view that the 'hidden self' (النفس الخفية) is the substance (الكيان), and that knowing this leads to knowing God. Maturidi does not explicitly endorse this view.

Key Terms
جهم
تغير الأحوال

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that God can acquire new attributes over time.
Correction: Maturidi argues that acquiring attributes after not having them implies change, which is a characteristic of contingent beings, not the eternal God.

Checkpoint
What is the basis for invalidating Jahm's statement?
A) God is one.
B) God cannot change.
C) God is all-powerful.
D) God is invisible.

Key Takeaway
Maturidi invalidates the view that God acquires attributes because change implies createdness and cannot be attributed to the eternal God.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you truly believe that God is perfect and complete without needing any change, or have you ever caught yourself thinking that God's knowledge or mercy might increase based on your actions? What does that imply about your understanding of His eternal perfection?
?
If God never changes, why do you still expect Him to treat you differently based on your deeds when His attributes are constant? What needs to change—not God's wrath or mercy, but your own soul?
?
When you ask Allah for something, do you think it changes Him? How does this truth about God's immutability (that He is always knowing, always powerful, always agent, always speaker) reshape the way you should supplicate—not to change Him, but to change yourself?

Evidence from Choice and the Hidden Self
Maturidi presents an argument from choice and attributes, and mentions the view of the hidden self.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Maturidi uses the concept of choice and the bearing of attributes to argue that God is not like blind natures, and the alternative view about the hidden self.

Core Idea
God's attributes are by choice, not by nature, and knowing the hidden self may lead to knowing God.

After invalidating the view that God acquires attributes, Maturidi presents a positive argument: the possibility of states through choice and the bearing of high attributes show that God is the All-Knowing, not like blind natures. He also mentions a saying about the hidden self. This lesson covers these points.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage
Read the Arabic text, noting the argument about choice and the reference to the hidden self.
2
Understand the argument from choice
Maturidi argues that God's attributes like knowledge and power are by choice (ikhtiyār), not by nature (tabā'iʿ), because natures are incapable of choice and ignorant.
This distinguishes God from natural forces.
3
Grasp the bearing of opposites
God's bearing of good and evil and varying states indicates that the true director is beyond such potentiality and change.
This leads to the conclusion that everything is as He decrees.
4
Consider the hidden self view
Maturidi mentions a saying: whoever knows his hidden self (al-nafs al-khafiyya) knows his Lord, and the hidden self is the entity (kayān).
Maturidi does not explicitly endorse this view; he presents it as an opinion of some people.

According to Maturidi, what does the bearing of good and evil by God prove?
A) That God is all-powerful.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you face a difficult situation, do you react like a blind nature—automatically and without thought—or do you pause and make a choice that reflects God's attributes of knowledge and power? What would change if you consciously chose to act as one who bears His attributes?
?
Maturidi says God bears good and evil. Which of these in your life have you attributed to yourself, or to luck, or to others? Do you truly believe that everything in your life—including the hard things—comes from God's decree and turns you to Him alone?
?
'Whoever knows his hidden self knows his Lord.' Have you ever truly looked into your own hidden self—your deepest motivations, fears, and desires? What would you find there that points you to your absolute need for God?

Three Sources of Doubt
Enumerating the three sources of doubt that hinder belief.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the three sources of doubt that prevent people from accepting the existence of a creator according to Imam Maturidi.

Core Idea
Doubt arises from blind imitation, sensory observation, and false inference about the unseen.

Imam Maturidi begins by diagnosing the psychological and epistemological obstacles that block people from recognizing the Creator. He identifies three distinct sources of doubt that lead to disbelief or misguided beliefs. This lesson unpacks those three sources and shows how they misdirect the mind away from reflection on divine proofs.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on the three doubts
Read the Arabic passage carefully. Identify the three sources Maturidi lists.
The text explicitly says 'from three aspects' and then enumerates them.
2
Understand the first doubt: imitation (taqlid)
Notice that taqlid is described as following the familiar, leading to abandoning reflection and pursuing desires.
3
Explore the second doubt: sensory observation
The second doubt is based on observing change and generation in the material world, which leads some to think nature is self-sufficient.
This is linked to naturalism and the denial of a transcendent cause.
4
Grasp the third doubt: false inference
Some conclude that because they haven't witnessed creation, it is impossible; they mistakenly equate the unseen with the witnessed.
5
Reflect on the impact of these doubts
Consider how these three doubts lead to various false doctrines, setting the stage for the refutation that follows.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فرأيت الشبهة اعترضت البشر من أوجه ثلاثة: أحدها التقليد بمن ألفت نفسه الشبهة لمن تصبح إليه، فترك التفكر في الأدلة، وأقبل على أماني النفس صحبتهم والوصول إلى شهوات النفس، أو اتهاما لآرائهم أن تهى[؟] بهم إو إنمامهم وغيره من أسباب الشقاء حتى يبلغ بهم العياذ بشره النف[؟] عاداتها. والثاني نظر[؟] إلى الوجود مما يقع تحت الحواس فوجده يتقلب من حال بالمواد والأغذية، وتولد بعض عن بعض، وظنوا أن كون الأشياء شيء، والفروع لا عن أصل محال وجوده؛ لأنهم لم يعاينوا ذلك، الشاه[؟] هو دليل الغائب. ثم تفرقوا: فمنهم من يقول: على هذا أمر العالم في ... لكنهم اختلفوا: فمنهم من يجعله كذلك على ما بينا، من غير أن يكون له صنع وعلى هذا يخرج مذهب أصحاب الطبائع: إن التفاوت والاختلاف على اختلاف الطبائع وتفاصيلها، وسماها قوم هيولى، والتفاوت في الذي ذكرت على مثال الأصباغ: إنها تخرج على ألوان مختلفة بتفاوت المزاج واعتداله، وعلى ذلك جعلوا جوهر البشر من اعتدال الطبائع، والدواب من اضطرابه، وعلى هذا كل شيء. ومنهم من يرى أصله الأربع من الطبائع، ولكن لكل جوهر أصلا، والطبائع دخيلة فيها. ومنهم من شعله[؟] كذلك بالطبائع ويقول: هو واحد، ويجعله علة تكون العالم، فيوجب قدمه بوجوده، ويذهب في اثبات الصانع إلى اتساق الأشياء واتقانها؛ إذ ذلك لا يكون إلا بمدير عليم، إذ الطبع لا يرجع إلى قدرة، و به صلاح الأشياء، فقالوا بالصانع، ثم إذ هو كان في الأزل فأوجبوا كون العالم في الأزل على نحو اقتران الأشياء بعلاها، على أنه إذ كان العالم مواهبه و تعميه و تبعمه، وأنه قادر بذاته فثبت وجوده وكرمه بذاته، فيلزم كون الذي كرمه يوجبه، وقدرته توجده، ولا قوة إلا بالله ومنهم من يقول: هذا العالم كان عن أصل حدثت الصنعة فيه، اختلفوا: فمنهم من يجعل أصلة طينة، أحدث البارى منها هذا العال العالم. لكنتهم[؟] وقوم خعاونه[؟] النجوم والشمس بما كن يجرين دائبات، ويجريهان نشوء[؟] العالم. ويجعلون للجرى ابتداء بإحالة كون شيء بشيء إلى ما لا أول له. ومنهم من يجعلها تعترف[؟] فيها الأعراض، فمن ذلك تولد العالم، يسمونه من قبل هيولى، ويصفونه على ما يصف أهل التوحيد الصانع، ثم أبطلوا ذلك باحتمال قبول الأعراض وتغيره من حال إلى حال
Then I saw that doubt confronts humans from three aspects: one is imitation (taqlid) by one whose soul is familiar with doubt towards whom it inclines, so he abandons reflection on proofs and turns to the fantasies of the soul, their companionship and attaining the desires of the soul, or accusation of their opinions that [؟] to them [؟] and other causes of misery until it reaches with them, God forbid, the evil of [؟] their habits. The second is looking[؟] at existence of what falls under the senses and finding it changing from state to state with materials and nutrients, and some being generated from others, and they thought that the coming-to-be of things is something, and that branches without a root are impossible in existence because they did not witness that, the witnessed[؟] is the proof of the unseen. Then they divided: some say thus the matter of the world is... but they differed: among them is one who makes it as we have shown, without there being any act for it, and on this emerges the school of the naturalists (ashab al-taba'i'): that variation and difference are according to the difference of natures and their details, and some call it hyh (hyle). And the variation in what I mentioned is like the example of dyes: they come out in different colors according to variation and balance of temperament, and on that they made the essence of humans from balance of natures, and beasts from their imbalance, and on this everything. And among them is one who sees its origin as the four natures, but each essence has an origin, and the natures are incidental to them. And among them is one who [؟] likewise with natures and says it is one, and makes it the cause of the world's becoming, so he necessitates its pre-eternity by its existence, and goes in proving the Maker to the coherence and perfection of things; since that cannot be except with a wise manager, for nature does not return to power, and by it the welfare of things, so they affirmed the Maker. Then since He was in pre-eternity, they necessitated the world's existence in pre-eternity on the pattern of things' conjunction with their causes, so that since the world is His gifts and [؟] and [؟], and that He is powerful by His essence, so His existence and generosity are established by His essence, then it follows that what He grants necessitates it, and His power brings it into existence, and there is no power but by God. And among them is one who says: this world came from an origin in which work occurred, and they differed: among them is one who makes its origin clay, from which the Creator originated this world. But they[؟] and a people [؟] the stars and the sun as they run constantly, and they make arise[؟] the world. And they make for the motion a beginning by negating the existence of a thing from something to what has no beginning. And among them is one who makes it [؟] the accidents in it, and from that the world is generated, they call it before hyh (hyle), and they describe it as the people of tawhid describe the Maker; then they refuted that by its possibility of receiving accidents and its change from state to state.
Source pages p. 710

Commentary
This passage classifies the sources of doubt and refutes various false doctrines. Maturidi first identifies three obstacles: (1) blind imitation (taqlid) based on habit and desire, (2) sensory observation that leads to naturalism—observing change and generation but failing to see the need for a transcendent cause, and (3) the erroneous conclusion that the absence of witnessed creation implies no creator. He then describes the divergent sects: the materialists who attribute everything to natures (taba'i') and hyle; those who see natures as secondary; those who affirm a single cause but still necessitate pre-eternity; those who posit a primordial clay; and those who attribute the world to stars or to a substratum capable of receiving accidents. Their common error is rejecting a transcendent creator. The refutation is that any supposed eternal matter that undergoes change cannot be eternal, and that the order and coherence of the world demand a wise manager, not a blind nature.

Key Terms
تقليد
شبهة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that doubt about God’s existence arises only from intellectual arguments, not from psychological and observational factors.
Correction: Maturidi shows that doubt often stems from blind habit, sensory impressions, and flawed reasoning about the unseen.

Checkpoint
According to Maturidi, what is the first source of doubt?
A) Sensory observation of generation and decay
B) Blind imitation (taqlid) of familiar beliefs
C) The argument from evil
D) The complexity of nature

Key Takeaway
Three sources of doubt—imitation, sensory observation, and false inference—block people from accepting the truth of a Creator.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Which of these three sources of doubt do you find yourself falling into most often? When did you last abandon reflection on a matter of faith because it was easier to follow what you were used to? What desires or fears prevented you from seeking the truth?
?
Have you ever rejected something from the unseen (like the existence of angels or the afterlife) simply because you couldn't perceive it with your senses? How does Maturidi's warning about this error challenge your own assumptions?
?
Think of a time when you made a conclusion about God's existence based only on what you could see. Did you forget that the unseen is not bound by the rules of the witnessed? How will you guard against this fallacy from now on?

Refutation of Naturalists (Ashab al-Taba'i)
Describing and refuting the naturalist doctrine that explains all by natures.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's refutation of the naturalists who attribute all variation and change to innate natures and deny a transcendent creator.

Core Idea
Nature cannot account for order, coherence, or design—only a wise Manager can.

After outlining the three doubts, Maturidi describes the divergent sects that arose from them. One major group is the naturalists (ashab al-taba'i), who explain all differences in the world through the mixture and balance of elemental natures. This lesson examines their view and Maturidi's implicit refutation: that order and perfection cannot arise from blind nature, but only from a wise Creator.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on naturalists
Focus on the part where Maturidi explains the naturalists' doctrine and the analogy of dyes.
The text mentions 'the school of the naturalists' and the dye analogy.
2
Understand the naturalist position
Naturalists claim that all variation in the world results from differences in natures and their proportions, like dyes producing different colors.
3
Grasp Maturidi's refutation
The refutation is implicit: nature cannot produce order and coherence by itself; it requires a wise manager (mudabbir 'alim).
Look for the phrase 'لا يكون إلا بمدير عليم'.
4
Compare with true tawhid
Maturidi contrasts naturalism with the correct belief that a powerful, wise Creator is necessary.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فرأيت الشبهة اعترضت البشر من أوجه ثلاثة: أحدها التقليد بمن ألفت نفسه الشبهة لمن تصبح إليه، فترك التفكر في الأدلة، وأقبل على أماني النفس صحبتهم والوصول إلى شهوات النفس، أو اتهاما لآرائهم أن تهى[؟] بهم إو إنمامهم وغيره من أسباب الشقاء حتى يبلغ بهم العياذ بشره النف[؟] عاداتها. والثاني نظر[؟] إلى الوجود مما يقع تحت الحواس فوجده يتقلب من حال بالمواد والأغذية، وتولد بعض عن بعض، وظنوا أن كون الأشياء شيء، والفروع لا عن أصل محال وجوده؛ لأنهم لم يعاينوا ذلك، الشاه[؟] هو دليل الغائب. ثم تفرقوا: فمنهم من يقول: على هذا أمر العالم في ... لكنهم اختلفوا: فمنهم من يجعله كذلك على ما بينا، من غير أن يكون له صنع وعلى هذا يخرج مذهب أصحاب الطبائع: إن التفاوت والاختلاف على اختلاف الطبائع وتفاصيلها، وسماها قوم هيولى، والتفاوت في الذي ذكرت على مثال الأصباغ: إنها تخرج على ألوان مختلفة بتفاوت المزاج واعتداله، وعلى ذلك جعلوا جوهر البشر من اعتدال الطبائع، والدواب من اضطرابه، وعلى هذا كل شيء. ومنهم من يرى أصله الأربع من الطبائع، ولكن لكل جوهر أصلا، والطبائع دخيلة فيها. ومنهم من شعله[؟] كذلك بالطبائع ويقول: هو واحد، ويجعله علة تكون العالم، فيوجب قدمه بوجوده، ويذهب في اثبات الصانع إلى اتساق الأشياء واتقانها؛ إذ ذلك لا يكون إلا بمدير عليم، إذ الطبع لا يرجع إلى قدرة، و به صلاح الأشياء، فقالوا بالصانع، ثم إذ هو كان في الأزل فأوجبوا كون العالم في الأزل على نحو اقتران الأشياء بعلاها، على أنه إذ كان العالم مواهبه و تعميه و تبعمه، وأنه قادر بذاته فثبت وجوده وكرمه بذاته، فيلزم كون الذي كرمه يوجبه، وقدرته توجده، ولا قوة إلا بالله ومنهم من يقول: هذا العالم كان عن أصل حدثت الصنعة فيه، اختلفوا: فمنهم من يجعل أصلة طينة، أحدث البارى منها هذا العال العالم. لكنتهم[؟] وقوم خعاونه[؟] النجوم والشمس بما كن يجرين دائبات، ويجريهان نشوء[؟] العالم. ويجعلون للجرى ابتداء بإحالة كون شيء بشيء إلى ما لا أول له. ومنهم من يجعلها تعترف[؟] فيها الأعراض، فمن ذلك تولد العالم، يسمونه من قبل هيولى، ويصفونه على ما يصف أهل التوحيد الصانع، ثم أبطلوا ذلك باحتمال قبول الأعراض وتغيره من حال إلى حال
Then I saw that doubt confronts humans from three aspects: one is imitation (taqlid) by one whose soul is familiar with doubt towards whom it inclines, so he abandons reflection on proofs and turns to the fantasies of the soul, their companionship and attaining the desires of the soul, or accusation of their opinions that [؟] to them [؟] and other causes of misery until it reaches with them, God forbid, the evil of [؟] their habits. The second is looking[؟] at existence of what falls under the senses and finding it changing from state to state with materials and nutrients, and some being generated from others, and they thought that the coming-to-be of things is something, and that branches without a root are impossible in existence because they did not witness that, the witnessed[؟] is the proof of the unseen. Then they divided: some say thus the matter of the world is... but they differed: among them is one who makes it as we have shown, without there being any act for it, and on this emerges the school of the naturalists (ashab al-taba'i'): that variation and difference are according to the difference of natures and their details, and some call it hyh (hyle). And the variation in what I mentioned is like the example of dyes: they come out in different colors according to variation and balance of temperament, and on that they made the essence of humans from balance of natures, and beasts from their imbalance, and on this everything. And among them is one who sees its origin as the four natures, but each essence has an origin, and the natures are incidental to them. And among them is one who [؟] likewise with natures and says it is one, and makes it the cause of the world's becoming, so he necessitates its pre-eternity by its existence, and goes in proving the Maker to the coherence and perfection of things; since that cannot be except with a wise manager, for nature does not return to power, and by it the welfare of things, so they affirmed the Maker. Then since He was in pre-eternity, they necessitated the world's existence in pre-eternity on the pattern of things' conjunction with their causes, so that since the world is His gifts and [؟] and [؟], and that He is powerful by His essence, so His existence and generosity are established by His essence, then it follows that what He grants necessitates it, and His power brings it into existence, and there is no power but by God. And among them is one who says: this world came from an origin in which work occurred, and they differed: among them is one who makes its origin clay, from which the Creator originated this world. But they[؟] and a people [؟] the stars and the sun as they run constantly, and they make arise[؟] the world. And they make for the motion a beginning by negating the existence of a thing from something to what has no beginning. And among them is one who makes it [؟] the accidents in it, and from that the world is generated, they call it before hyh (hyle), and they describe it as the people of tawhid describe the Maker; then they refuted that by its possibility of receiving accidents and its change from state to state.
Source pages p. 710

Commentary
This passage classifies the sources of doubt and refutes various false doctrines. Maturidi first identifies three obstacles: (1) blind imitation (taqlid) based on habit and desire, (2) sensory observation that leads to naturalism—observing change and generation but failing to see the need for a transcendent cause, and (3) the erroneous conclusion that the absence of witnessed creation implies no creator. He then describes the divergent sects: the materialists who attribute everything to natures (taba'i') and hyle; those who see natures as secondary; those who affirm a single cause but still necessitate pre-eternity; those who posit a primordial clay; and those who attribute the world to stars or to a substratum capable of receiving accidents. Their common error is rejecting a transcendent creator. The refutation is that any supposed eternal matter that undergoes change cannot be eternal, and that the order and coherence of the world demand a wise manager, not a blind nature.

Key Terms
طبائع
هيولى

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that naturalistic explanations (like modern science) are sufficient to explain order without a creator.
Correction: Maturidi shows that even naturalists are compelled to admit a wise manager when they see coherence; nature alone is blind and powerless.

Checkpoint
What analogy do the naturalists use to explain variation?
A) The analogy of a builder and a house
B) The analogy of dyes producing different colors
C) The analogy of a seed growing into a tree
D) The analogy of a clock with gears

Key Takeaway
Naturalists fail to see that order and perfection demand a transcendent wise Manager, not blind natures.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever attributed the order in your life—your health, provision, or even your faith—to mere chance or natural causes? Where exactly do you see this subtle naturalism in your own heart?
?
If nature is blind, how can you trust the reasoning from your own brain, which is part of that same nature? What does this force you to conclude about the origin of your intellect and certainty?
?
The naturalists admitted a Manager but still denied His exclusive sovereignty over time. Is there any area in your life where you acknowledge God's wisdom yet live as if things run on autopilot? What step can you take today to consciously attribute each moment to Him alone?

Sects and Disagreements
intermediate
7 min
Refutation of Erroneous Theistic Sects
Refuting theistic sects with erroneous views on the world's origination.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's refutation of sects that affirm a creator but make erroneous claims about the world's origination, such as pre-eternity, astral causation, or a primordial substratum.

Core Idea
Affirming a creator is not enough; one must also affirm that the world is originated and contingent, not co-eternal or caused by stars.

Maturidi now turns to groups who, unlike outright materialists, do affirm a Maker but introduce errors about the world's relationship to Him. Some say the world is co-eternal with God, others attribute its generation to stars or a primordial clay, and yet others posit a substratum (hyle) capable of receiving accidents. This lesson exposes their contradictions and shows why only the tawhid view—that God creates the world freely from nothing—is correct.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on theistic errors
Identify the different groups Maturidi mentions after the naturalists.
2
Understand the first error: co-eternal world
Some say that since God is eternal, His act of creation must also be eternal, so the world is co-eternal with Him.
Maturidi refutes this by implication: God's power and generosity do not necessitate an eternal effect.
3
Grasp the second error: astral causation
Others attribute the world's origin to celestial bodies like stars and the sun, which they see as perpetually moving.
This is a form of astral determinism.
4
Examine the third error: hyle (primordial matter)
Some posit a pre-existing substratum (hyle) that receives accidents, describing it with attributes similar to the Creator, but then refute themselves by noting its changeability.
Anything that changes cannot be eternal or divine.
5
Reflect on the overarching refutation
All these errors fail because they do not fully accept that the world is contingent, originated, and dependent on a transcendent Creator who is not subject to change.

Source Text

What is the common error of the groups described in this passage?
A) They deny the existence of God entirely

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?

?

The Adherents of Natures
Maturidi describes the first materialist school: natures determine all diversity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the first materialist view presented by Imam al-Maturidi, which attributes all diversity in the world to the diversity of natures (taba'i') and their mixtures, and rejects direct divine action.

Core Idea
Nature, not a Creator, explains the diversity of the world.

Imam al-Maturidi begins his survey of materialist views by presenting the school of the 'adherents of natures.' This group denies any creative act and instead explains all differences—from human existence to animal forms—through the interplay of natural elements. Their analogy of dyes mixing to produce colors illustrates their core principle.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice how the author presents this view as a deviation from the true belief in creation.
2
Identify the core claim
What is the main claim of the adherents of natures? They say difference and diversity come from the diversity of natures.
3
Understand the dye analogy
The text compares diversity to dyes of different colors resulting from mixture. Reflect on why this analogy is used to explain natural variation.
4
Apply the analogy
According to this view, the human substance results from a balance of natures, while beasts come from imbalance. How does this fit the dye analogy?
5
Consider the implication
What does this view imply about the role of a Creator? The passage says 'without any act of creation' – so what is the role of God?

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
لكنهم اختلفوا : فمنهم من يجعله كذلك على ما بينا ، من غير أن يكون له صنع وعلى هذا يخرج مذهب أصحاب الطبائع : إن التفاوت والاختلاف على اختلاف الطبائع وتفاصيلها ، وسماها قوم هيولى ، والتفاوت فى الذي ذكرت على مثال الأصباغ : إنها تخرج على ألوان مختلفة بتفاوت المزاج واعتداله ، وعلى ذلك جعلوا جوهر البشر من اعتدال الطبائع ، والدواب من اضطرابه ، وعلى هذا كل شيء . ومنهم من يرى أصله الأربع من الطبائع ، ولكن لكل جوهر أصلا ، والطبائع دخيلة فيها. ومنهم من جعله كذلك بالطبائع ويقول : هو واحد ، ويجعله علة تكون العالم ، فيوجب قدمه بوجوده ، ويذهب في اثبات الصانع إلى اتساق الأشياء واتقانها ؛ إذ ذلك لا يكون إلا بمدير عليم ، إذ الطبع لا يرجع إلى قدرة ، و به صلاح الأشياء ، فقالوا بالصانع ، ثم إذ هو كان في الأزل فأوجبوا كون العالم فى الأزل على نحو اقتران الأشياء بعلاها ، على أنه إذ كان العالم مواهبه و تعميه و تبعمه ، وأنه قادر بذاته فثبت وجوده وكرمه بذاته ، فيلزم كون الذي كرمه يوجبه ، وقدرته توجده ، ولا قوة إلا بالله
But they differed: some of them made it as we described, without any act of creation, and upon this goes the school of the adherents of natures (taba'i'): that difference and diversity are according to the diversity of natures and their details, and some people called them matter (hayula). The diversity in what I mentioned is like the example of dyes: they come out in different colors due to the difference and balance of the mixture. Upon that, they considered the substance of humans from the balance of natures, and of beasts from their imbalance, and upon this everything. And among them is one who thinks its origin is the four natures, but each substance has an origin, and the natures are incidental in them. And among them is one who made it likewise by natures and says: it is one, and he makes it the cause of the world's coming-to-be, thereby necessitating its eternity by its existence. He goes in proving the Maker to the coherence and perfection of things, for that cannot be except by a knowing Manager, for nature does not refer to power, and through Him is the good order of things. So they affirmed the Maker. Then when He was eternal, they necessitated the world's coming-to-be in eternity in the manner of the connection of things with their causes, upon that when the world was [??], and that He is powerful by His essence, so His existence and generosity are established by His essence. Therefore, it follows that what He has bestowed necessitates it, and His power brings it into existence. And there is no power except with God.
Source pages p. 711

Commentary
This passage classifies and describes various materialist views about the origin of the world. The author, Imam al-Maturidi, begins by noting that those who reject divine action in the world still differ among themselves. The first group described are those who attribute all diversity to the four natures (taba'i') and their combinations, comparing them to dyes that produce different colors based on mixture. They believe that human essence is from a balance of natures, while animals are from imbalance. Then he mentions a subgroup that considers the four natures as origins but each substance has its own origin with natures being incidental. Another group posits a single nature as the cause of the world, necessitating the world's eternity, and then tries to prove the Maker through the coherence and perfection of things, arguing that nature does not have power. They end up affirming a Maker but then claim the world is eternal, which leads to a contradiction: if the Maker is powerful by essence, then His power would necessitate the world's existence eternally. The author concludes this section with the statement 'There is no power except with God', indicating the insufficiency of such views.

Key Terms
طبائع (taba'i')
هيولى (hayula)
أصباغ (dyes)
مزاج (mixture)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That this view completely denies any divine being.
Correction: The passage only denies divine action in creation; it does not necessarily deny existence of a Maker, as later groups affirm a Maker.

Checkpoint
According to the adherents of natures, what determines the difference between humans and beasts?
A) The balance of natures
B) The will of a creator
C) Pure chance
D) The four elements

Key Takeaway
The first materialist view attributes all diversity to the balance and imbalance of natures, excluding any direct creative act.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you witness the diversity in nature—humans, animals, plants—do you immediately see the direct hand of Allah creating each one, or do you tend to default to natural explanations like 'evolution' or 'chemistry'? What one thought or action today can you change to consciously acknowledge Allah as the active Creator every moment?
?
The adherents of natures reduce humans to a mere balanced mixture, stripping them of any special status or purpose. Do you ever live as if your existence is just a product of random natural forces? What does it mean for your daily choices to truly believe that Allah created you with a specific purpose and will hold you accountable?

Sects and Disagreements
